#Series:** Enter the System - Leg Locks
**Instructor:** John Danaher  
**Volumes:** 1–8  
**Context:** This is the groundbreaking instructional that reshaped modern no-gi jiu-jitsu by presenting lower body submissions as a complete, systematic approach rather than isolated moves. Danaher begins by explaining the centrality of ashi garami positions (single-leg X, outside ashi, cross ashi, 50/50, and saddle/inside sankaku) as control systems that immobilize the opponent’s hips and legs. He details how to enter leg entanglements safely from standing, guard, and passing scenarios, emphasizing kuzushi (off-balancing) and structured entries over opportunism. The core of the system revolves around the heel hook as the decisive submission, with mechanics, finishing details, and safety considerations thoroughly broken down. Danaher also maps out secondary submissions (straight ankle locks, toe holds, kneebars) as complementary weapons when the heel hook is unavailable. A major focus is positional control and retention, showing how to maintain dominance within leg entanglements, cycle between positions, and counter common escapes. Finally, he integrates the leg lock game with upper-body attacks and traditional jiu-jitsu, ensuring it is not a “separate art” but part of a unified grappling strategy. The result is a complete leg lock framework that balances positional safety, high-percentage finishing, and strategic integration with the rest of jiu-jitsu.


## Volume 1

(0:04 - 0:22)
Hi, my name is John Danaher. I'm going to teach elements of my leg lock system, which I taught to a group of talented students that I had in New York City, and which they used with spectacular success in international level grappling. I'm joined by a very good friend of mine, Bernardo Faria.

(0:22 - 0:25)
Hello, John. Thank you. And my assistant, Karl Massara.

(0:30 - 0:55)
My intention in these videos is not simply to offer a collection of techniques. The world is full of instructional videos where a talented teacher or athlete shows a collection of moves. My intention, rather, is to show a holistic approach to leg locking that takes an athlete from the very beginning of an engagement, from the time of slapping hands with an opponent, all the way through, hopefully, to a successful submission hold.

(0:56 - 1:25)
You're going to learn that there's a sequence of steps which encapsulate both techniques and tactics, which go in predictable fashion, leading from a neutral start to a decisive conclusion. Much of what I teach is going to be put in the backdrop of four mechanical elements, which form the basis of my entire teaching methodology. The first of these is well known to many people, the idea of lever and fulcrum.

(1:25 - 1:52)
But I want to go further into the notion of lever and fulcrum and get more precise about exactly what we mean when we use these terms in the context that we get to. Let's start off with a way of thinking about lever and fulcrum. Most people are aware that in the use of lever and fulcrum, I want to manipulate the end of the lever.

(1:53 - 2:09)
I want to maximise my leverage by positioning or applying my forces at the end of a lever. Much less is talked about with regards to the fulcrum. A fulcrum, its value, can be judged according to three distinct elements.

(2:09 - 2:22)
First, the height of the fulcrum, the hardness of the fulcrum. These are critical concepts. Let's have a look in the case of Judy Gattame, and then we'll bring it back to Leglocks later on.

(2:23 - 2:35)
The armbar, Judy Gattame, is well known to even beginning students at Jiu-Jitsu. It involves, obviously, a lever. When I go to work, I always want to work at the end of the lever.

(2:35 - 3:01)
So often I see people working down here, the short end of the lever, when in fact they should be working up high at the end of the lever. The fulcrum, most people use the middle of their thighs as the breaking fulcrum. So they'll take the hand back in the direction where the lever arm is taken through the middle of the thighs, and they're taught to pinch their knees as a means of creating a fulcrum over which to break the arm.

(3:02 - 3:12)
There's some problems associated with this, you can see at the start. No matter how strong I flex my legs, the muscles are not particularly hard. There's a problem with the hardness of the fulcrum.

(3:13 - 3:25)
Picture a situation where I'm trying to break a pencil. If I put a pencil over a very hard fulcrum, it's extremely easy to break it. But if I put that same pencil over a very soft fulcrum, now it's difficult.

(3:25 - 3:48)
So I want to maximise the hardness of the fulcrum. A simple way for me to do this is instead of using my inner thigh muscles, I'll use the hard bones of the hip. So when I go to take someone's arm, I want to make sure I take his right arm over my right hip, so that I use the hard bones of the hip as a breaking fulcrum, rather than the soft inner thigh muscles.

(3:48 - 4:01)
So this is the practical sense in which we use the hardness principle of fulcrums. We always want to maximise the hardness of the fulcrum. We'll soon see that the same thing can be done in the case of leg locking.

(4:01 - 4:12)
The other element is the height of the fulcrum. The lower the fulcrum, the harder it is for me to break a pencil over, for example. So I want to do everything I can to raise the height of a given fulcrum.

(4:13 - 4:29)
In the case of judogitami, this is done by ensuring that my feet come back towards my opponent in a back-heel fashion. I can also take one leg and raise the knee, so that my hip is raised relative to the floor. And as a result, we get very, very strong breaking effects.

(4:30 - 4:45)
I can also do it by changing the very nature of the judogitami. I can, for example, put a foot underneath my opponent's head, and this will artificially raise the height of the fulcrum on the right-hand side. And as a result, we get much stronger breaking effects in a shorter distance.

(4:45 - 4:59)
Why? Because I've raised the height of the fulcrum. I'm maximising the hardness of the fulcrum by taking over the hip bone rather than the soft adductor muscles, and I'm raising the height. As a result, I get better breaking leverage.

(5:03 - 5:23)
Considerations like these are extremely important when it comes time to apply the principles of lever and fulcrum in any jiu-jitsu context. So that's our first major thing that we're going to be pushing as we go through this entire DVD series, the idea of lever and fulcrum. Paying particular attention to the idea of maximising the use of the fulcrum through height and hardness.

(5:24 - 5:42)
The other element, of course, in fulcrums is placement. If I place the fulcrum in an inefficient spot, I have to do more mechanical, apply greater force to get the break. So we're going to be paying a lot of attention to the placement of the fulcrum, the hardness of the fulcrum, and the height of the fulcrum.

(5:42 - 6:13)
These will be critical concepts as we work our way through the DVD. A second key element in terms of this pure mechanics, which will play a very, very important role throughout this DVD, is the idea of directionality of force. So often I see talented students doubling or tripling the amount of work they have to do to get a task done by applying forces in the wrong directions, or in less than optimal directions.

(6:14 - 6:29)
Let's look at a common sense example that you're all familiar with, a basic butterfly sweep. For any basic sweeping situation, I want to apply forces in a given direction. Some of those directions will be more efficient than others.

(6:30 - 6:53)
If I try to sweep Carlos Carlos directly to the side, I'm sweeping into one of his bases of support, especially when he starts to extend the leg up. As a result, I'm having to do tremendous work because I'm pushing into my opponent's base of support. But just a simple change in direction to a forward angle means that I get much better results.

(6:53 - 7:22)
It's not that I got majorly stronger, it's just that I employed the same amount of force in a more efficient direction. Maximising our use of force through applying forces in the appropriate direction is going to be an extremely important theme throughout this entire DVD series. A third critical theme is the idea of Kizushi, or off-balancing.

(7:22 - 7:43)
In theoretical terms, Kizushi is really just an extension of labour and fulcrum, but it's such an important subset of that study in bottom position that I actually separated it into distinct entities. I didn't do this study in and of itself. So for example, I had someone standing over me in butterfly guard.

(7:44 - 8:05)
As long as he's in good stance, working against me, it's difficult for me to do anything effective on someone who's in good stance and in good balance. But the moment I start to offset my opponent's balance, I get the two things that I need in order to play a strong submissions game. These are the idea of extension and movement.

(8:05 - 8:25)
If you give me an opponent who's moving and extended, I can submit him. But if my opponent's contracted, it's hard for me to go into my submissions game. But the moment I start getting people out of balance and disturbing their base of support by taking their centre of gravity outside of their base of support, getting hands to the floor, et cetera, et cetera, and suddenly where I get extension, now I can go into submissions.

(8:26 - 8:45)
I've broken his stance, I've disconnected my opponent, and now the submissions game starts to become easy. From here, it won't be a difficult thing for me to fly my legs up into winning positions. So this will form another key element, a key theme that will run throughout this DVD, the idea of Kizushi.

(8:45 - 9:28)
Whenever we're in bottom position, regardless of bottom position we're in, anytime we find ourselves underneath our opponent, we're always looking to get their centre of gravity over their base of support, disturb their balance, and create extension and motion. If I have those two things, I can enter into my opponent's legs repeatedly and get to a winning. Now a fourth key theme that we work with, and one which I don't think has really been emphasised at all in Jiu-Jitsu instruction, but which I believe is absolutely one of the most important mechanical features of Jiu-Jitsu, is the use of wedges.

(9:30 - 9:49)
Wedges are really just a species of inclined plane in physics. They can be used for many different tasks, but in the context of Jiu-Jitsu, the number one task they're employed for is to immobilise people. Often, when we think in Jiu-Jitsu, we talk about immobilisation in terms of body weight.

(9:50 - 10:07)
In fact, wedges are by far and away the most important form of immobilisation. If I can immobilise an opponent, then I can work my own body around them and ultimately control them and cement them. If I can't immobilise someone, if I can't directly control their movement, I'm not going to cement anybody.

(10:08 - 10:20)
The use of wedges is easily demonstrated by a simple experiment. Bernardo, you're going to the power platform, start control body. Let's do an experiment here.

(10:20 - 10:30)
Bernardo Ferrer is on top of his opponent. Bernardo is going to take his hands and put them behind his back. Both hands? Your hands behind your own back.

(10:30 - 10:40)
Now you're going to take your knees off the floor. Good. Right now, Bernardo Ferrer has a significant amount of his body weight directly on the chest at Kama-Sara.

(10:40 - 10:56)
There's a sense in which this is a good pin. He's chest to chest and a large percentage of his body weight is directly working down upon his opponent. And yet, if he maintained his hands behind his back and I asked Kama-Sara to slide out and put him in guard, it's Kota-Kama.

(10:58 - 11:06)
It was the easiest thing in the world. Why? Because Bernardo had no wedges around his opponent. Now let's contrast that.

(11:06 - 11:25)
This time, I'm just going to put a wedge underneath one side of my opponent's body and a wedge with my knee on the other side. You can clearly see, because my hand is on the floor, I have no body weight on top of Kama-Sara. There is no body weight there.

(11:25 - 11:40)
But when Karl goes to put me back in guard, he physically can't do it. It wasn't the body weight that held him down, it was the use of wedges. In fact, this time he applies the conventional pin.

(11:45 - 12:01)
Now, Bernardo has wedges on his opponent. When he applies his body weight now, when Karl goes to escape and put him back in guard, it's significantly more difficult for him. Why? What changed? Well, earlier he had no wedges around Kama-Sara's body.

(12:01 - 12:14)
Now he does. And in addition, he's reinforced him with body weight. The use of wedges to immobilise an opponent is probably the single most important thing that we're going to be pushing in this DVD series.

(12:15 - 12:31)
Now, how we use wedges is extremely important. Ashi-Gurami, the basis of this entire Lego DVD, is essentially a system of wedges around your opponent's legs and hips. But unlike a conventional pin, it's not reinforced by body weight.

(12:32 - 12:52)
When I go into an Ashi-Gurami position, I have no body weight on top of my opponent. The only thing holding him in is the wedges. Given a certain kind of Ashi-Gurami, because I have one form of Ashi-Gurami, the wedges that form around my opponent are not reinforced by body weight.

(12:54 - 13:17)
The only way I can reinforce them is mechanically by locking legs in various positions, etc. Now, as much as we like wedges, we like even more the notion of reinforced wedges. The question is, how are we going to reinforce them? Conventionally, in a pin, it's done by body weight, as we saw with an Aritabara earlier.

(13:18 - 13:27)
In the case of Ashi-Gurami, that's not the case. Since both of us have our bodies on the floor, neither one of us has weight on the other. So the system of reinforcement has to be very different.

(13:28 - 13:47)
Typically, it's done by locking legs. You know, triangles, or sankakus, or just simply crossing our feet. This idea of reinforced wedges and using wedges to immobilise opponents so that we can control them prior to submitting them is going to be one of the absolute most important themes that runs throughout this DVD series.

(13:52 - 14:10)
So right from the back, we're going to see that there are four keynote themes that run throughout this entire DVD series, which we will come back to time and again. These are the idea of Kizushi, or off-balancing. This is extremely important anytime you're in the bottom position.

(14:11 - 14:26)
Kizushi, or off-balancing, will create movement and extension and weaken my opponent's stance. It will create opportunities to go into locks on strong defensive opponents, opening up opportunities to go in and get to a leg lock finish. We've seen the idea of lever and foreground.

(14:26 - 14:52)
We've seen that we're going to insist on the idea of maximising the use of the lever by consistently hunting for the end of the lever and the manipulation of the end of the lever. At the same time, we're going to maximise our use of the foreground by insisting upon the height, the hardness, and the position of the foreground to make sure we get the greatest amount of mechanical work done with the least amount of effort. We've looked at the idea of directionality and force.

(14:52 - 15:11)
Every time we go to apply force in a leg lock situation, we're going to have to insist on the idea of the correct directions of force. Without that, we're always going to have substandard results with our leg lock in the end. And then arguably the most important of the four, the idea of wedging.

(15:12 - 15:32)
We're going to look at the idea of wedges as a means of immobilising an opponent, gaining control of their movement so that we can hold them long enough to go into a leg lock win. We've seen the idea of reinforcing wedges is extremely important. The best forms of wedge always involve wedges on both sides of the human body.

(15:32 - 15:54)
If I'm holding Carlos Araudel on the side of his face, and I have wedges on one side of his body, that will prove inadequate when he simply slides away from me and he's gone. I have wedges, but I only have them on one side of his body. When I apply a wedge on both sides of the body, now when he goes to move, I can easily immobilise him.

(15:54 - 16:09)
Okay, so we need wedges on both sides of the body. At the same time, we want to reinforce those wedges to the greatest degree possible. In the case of conventional fins, they're usually reinforced by body weight, but we can't do that in astrodynamics since they're both sitting in front of each other.

(16:09 - 16:28)
The wedges have to be reinforced by biomechanical means, usually cross feet or triangle legs. These four themes will run throughout our BDD series. Our intention is to show the leg lock system in its entirety.

(16:29 - 16:53)
The only way we can start that project is by giving a brief overview of what the leg lock system is. Most people teach a leg lock, they teach it as a simple move, a heel hook, a figure four toehold or what have you. What I want to do is to teach you an entire set of skills, what I call the six essential skills of heel hooking from start to finish.

(16:55 - 17:07)
It's important we have a general sense of what we're trying to accomplish here. Let's make some simple distinctions right from the start. The leg lock system has a definite structure.

(17:07 - 17:38)
There's the general system, the system overall, which is part of the jiu-jitsu system overall. For zoi jiu-jitsu on the one hand, there's a leg lock system within that and within the general leg lock system, there are three subsystems. Probably the single best way that we can start the ball rolling is to talk about what is the general leg lock system and what are the six essential skills of heel hooking.

(17:39 - 17:53)
Let's understand first, let's bring in some kind of precision when we talk about a leg lock system. Leg locking is just a generic term. It means any form of lock that is applied to my opponent's legs.

(17:55 - 18:06)
To be honest with you, what I teach is not a leg locking system per se. It's an ashi garami system. Remember, there are many forms of leg lock that don't involve ashi garami at all.

(18:06 - 18:21)
In fact, my friend Renato Fari is famous for applying a certain kind of leg lock which doesn't involve ashi garami at all. Really, what I teach is an ashi garami system. Remember, there are many forms of leg lock that don't involve ashi garami at all.

(18:22 - 18:48)
What I did is I focused upon what I believe are the most high percentage forms of leg locking when they're allowed, at least in competition, which is heel hooking. For mechanical reasons, which I'll outline later, I believe that heel hooking is probably the most efficient way of attacking an experienced opponent's legs, one with the highest percentage chance of success. And I believe the statistics back me up on this.

(18:48 - 19:13)
If you look at international competition where heel hooks are permitted, you will see that for every time people tap to other forms of leg lock, they'll tap 100 times to heel hooks. So we've got, on the one hand, the general leg lock system, which encapsulates six distinct skills which you will have to master and which I will teach in the course of this DVD. Within that general system, there are three distinct subsystems.

(19:15 - 19:45)
These are, I'll demonstrate with Kama Saru, three families of ashi garami. The way of breaking ashi garami down, which I'm going to show you, is the one which I taught to all of my students. There are different ways I could teach an ashi garami system, but it's my belief that the three subsystem method, which I use with my students and which I'm going to use with you now, is probably the clearest and easiest way to understand what would otherwise be a very large amount of information.

(19:46 - 20:02)
The first family, the first subsystem, are so-called straight ashi garamis. A straight ashi garami is any situation where two of my legs... You should know, by the way, ashi garami is a generic term. It just means entangled legs.

(20:02 - 20:22)
Typically, it refers to a situation where two of my legs control one of my opponent's legs. So there's a sense of mechanical advantage that I should always have some kind of control advantage over my opponent because two of my legs are controlling one of his. So even if he were considerably larger and stronger than me, in theory, at least two of my legs should be stronger than one of my opponent's legs.

(20:24 - 20:42)
There are three main forms of ashi garami. The first are the so-called straight ashi garami attacks, where Kama Saru's left leg goes over my right hip so that his leg stays on one side of my centre line. So within this family, there are many variations.

(20:43 - 21:05)
So for example, I might have what many people refer to as a standing ashi garami, or a primi ashi garami, as I call it. From here, I could bring my feet to the inside, like so. Still, the leg is going across my right hip, so we're still within the framework of a straight ashi garami system, but I've changed my ashi garami now to a senkaku with my feet locked inside.

(21:06 - 21:19)
I could have my feet locked on the outside. Still, we're in a straight ashi garami situation, but the nature of my ashi garami has changed. So we saw three variations of a straight ashi garami.

(21:20 - 21:32)
This stands in contrast to a cross ashi garami. In a cross ashi garami, my opponent's leg goes across my centre line. His right leg goes across my right hip.

(21:32 - 21:45)
And within this context, there are many different variations. I can have my feet close together like so, inside my opponent's legs. I can have my feet over my opponent's legs, over the far hip.

(21:46 - 21:59)
I can have my feet locked in a triangle with my feet to the inside. I can have my feet locked in a triangle with a foot to the outside. These are all cross ashi garami, but different variations within that family.

(22:00 - 22:18)
A third form of ashi garami is the so-called reverse ashi garami. This is the only form of ashi garami where I face the same direction as my opponent. Every other ashi garami, I face towards my opponent.

(22:18 - 22:29)
In this one, I face away from my opponent. From here, I have one leg caught between mine. In this framework, there are many variations, but they're all held together by a common thread.

(22:29 - 22:40)
The idea that we both face the same direction. I face away from my opponent in the same direction he's facing. This stands in contrast to all the other forms of ashi garami where I always face my opponent.

(22:42 - 23:09)
We have then a general system of leg locking which encapsulates six distinct skills that are put into a slightly flexible sequence. Within that general framework, there are three distinct subsystems. Straight ashi garami family, cross ashi garami family, and reverse ashi garami family.

(23:10 - 23:45)
You will be expected to learn and master all three of these subsystems in the context of a general system if you're going to call yourself competent in leg locking. We looked at the idea of a general theory of leg locking. We saw that it encapsulates six distinct skills, six essential skills, without which you can't really call yourself a competent leg locker, at least with heel hooks.

(23:45 - 24:14)
The first of these, and the element which starts off or gets the ball rolling with leg locking, is always step number one, attaining a grip and inside position. Everything in the sport of jiu jitsu begins with a grip. In the case of ashi garami based leg locks, the other element is inside positioning of my feet.

(24:15 - 25:52)
Or my knees. Let's have a look at the idea of grip and inside position as the first essential skill in our general leg lock system. We need to be highly competent in the use of ashi garami in both top and bottom position.

We're going to start in bottom position. That's where the practical study of ashi garami and heel hooking is going to begin. Before we can begin that, we need to study the idea of getting some form of working grip upon my opponent.

Until I grip my opponent and come to grips with him, I can't control him. One of the big themes of our DVD is the idea of gaining control over opponent's movement and then from there working our way into submission holds. But everything begins with control.

The only way one human being can control another is through some kind of physical contact. In the case of jiu jitsu, that means through grip. Gripping is the first skill that you will get to utilise in any competition or sparring scenario.

Until you get to grips with your opponent, nothing is going to happen. The question is, how are we going to use grip in the most effective fashion? A large part of that will depend upon how my opponent is positioned as he goes to make contact with me. We're going to start in a situation where my opponent is on two knees in front of me and I'm working from underneath from a seated butterfly guard situation.

(25:57 - 26:15)
One of the most important forms of gripping for advantage that we can work with is the idea of a two-on-one grip. If I simply grip my opponent in a generic fashion like so, I haven't really exercised any kind of advantage. There's nothing intrinsically advantageous about my grip.

(26:15 - 28:08)
One of my hands is gripping one of my opponent's hands. If he proves to be stronger than me, he can pull away and move on. Moreover, the very nature of gripping has to be understood here.

The human hand is set up in such a way that it has strong zones and weak zones. The weakness of any grip when I go to grab my opponent's hands and wrists is through the area between my thumb and my fingers. That represents the weak zone of my grip.

If I hold my hand like so, you can see a space between my thumb and my fingers. That's the space of escape for my opponent. If he pulls his wrist in the direction my thumb is pointing, he will easily escape from my grip.

If he went the opposite way, he would never escape because he's going into my hand. If he goes vertically upwards, he's going into my thumb. If he goes vertically downwards, he's going into the strength of my fingers.

If he goes out in the direction my thumb points, he escapes easily. He's exploiting the very nature of the human hand and its weaknesses. Whenever I go to employ a two-on-one grip upon my opponent, I have to cover the weak zone of my hands.

The only way I can do this is through the use of two-on-one gripping. So, when someone comes in and goes to escape from a given grip, I know ahead of time that's the zone of weakness. It's revealed by the direction of my thumb.

My thumb points towards the weak zone of the grip. What I need to do is cover that zone of weakness with the other hand. The easiest way for you to understand this is that I always want my thumbs pointing in opposite directions.

So, when Kyle Massara goes to pull away from me and I put a second hand on here, that's the weak zone shown by my left thumb pointing out here. I cover it with the right hand. Now, when Kyle Massara goes to pull away from me, it's shockingly difficult.

(28:09 - 29:02)
For the first time, I'm using one hand to get an initial grip and I'm covering the weaknesses of that hand with my other. So, I have two hands on one in a way where it's quite difficult for my opponent to pull away from me. When Kyle Massara goes to pull away, I don't want to sit still and resist his pull.

Kyle, just explosively pull your elbow back to your own body. He can eventually pull out. I never want to stay still against my opponent's pull.

Let's look at the result. Eventually, he'll pull no matter how tightly I hold on. Whenever someone goes to pull, I want to move with their pull.

So, if he goes to pull, I want to pull my body in so I shorten the distance between us. Remember, if I'm pulling back and he pulls in, that's the inevitable result. Once I get my grip, I must be sensitive to the duration of his resistance.

(29:03 - 30:22)
So, I catch a grip. I see him go to pull away and I move with the pull. As a result, my grip is retained and from here, I can start to play my attacking game.

Another example of this would be when Kyle Massara was standing. This time, I use a different form of two-on-one gripping. I start with a down grip.

My thumb points downwards. Now, you should know by now where is the zone of weakness. It's always revealed by the direction of my thumb.

If Kyle Massara pulls his hand in that direction, he easily escapes. If he tries to push towards me, my hand easily holds him. If he tries to go vertically up, I easily hold him.

If he goes vertically down, again I can hold him. But when he goes in the direction my thumb is pointing, he easily escapes. So, two-on-one gripping.

One thumb pointing against the other. He goes to pull away now. It's exceptionally difficult.

One of my hands is asserting the grip and the second hand is covering the weakness of the first hand. When I use my hands in this two-on-one fashion, you can create extraordinary gripping pressure. Often, when students visit the gym or we're in competition and opponents come up to talk to us afterwards, a common theme that they all express is that my students have extraordinary grip strength.

(30:23 - 31:54)
It's not that they have extraordinary grip strength. It's that they know what they're doing when they grip. Every time they go to make a grip, the first thing they do is they cover the weakness of the initial grip with the second hand.

This is a crucial, crucial theme in gripping and it's best done through the use of two-on-one grips. We'll be spending quite a bit of time on the idea of two-on-one grips. We're starting with what we call two-on-one wrist grips.

Here, I have a two-on-one down grip. My hand goes down towards the floor. I reinforce it with the second hand, making sure my thumbs point in opposite directions so that the weakness of one hand is covered by the other.

When Kamasara goes to pull away, it's shockingly difficult. Now we're going to add a second crucial concept to the idea of gripping, the concept of the immovable elbow. What is the immovable elbow? One of the strongest structures of the human body is when I bring my own elbow to my hips.

Let's do an experiment, Kamasara. We're going to link hands like so. I'm going to bring my elbow to my hip, just like so.

When Kamasara walks backwards and lifts, my whole body will come off the floor before my elbow leaves my hip. If you want to feel strong to another human being through grip, bring your elbow close to your own hip. This utilises all of the powerful left muscles in the back, some of the strongest muscles in the human body.

(31:54 - 33:03)
When Kamasara pulls me, I will lose my balance before my elbow comes off my hip. So I want to create a strong gripping structure. One of the best things I can do is, once I assert grips, is to bring my elbows to my own hips.

So when I work from a down position and I catch my opponent's hand, I not only reinforce one hand with the other, I close my elbows to my hips. When Kamasara goes to pull away from me, my whole body will move before the grip breaks. I can create the illusion of immense gripping strength.

Not because I'm strong, but because I'm gripping properly. I'm taking advantage of these subtle elements of gripping that prove to be so important when we enter into the leg lock game. We saw earlier that whenever someone goes to pull away from me, I never stay still and pull against them.

If I stay still and he yanks, yanks, yanks, yanks, eventually he's going to break out. Instead, standing square, when my opponent goes to pull away from me, I walk with them. I never resist a pull by pulling back and remaining stationary.

(33:04 - 35:37)
I move in the same direction as my opponent's pull. He's down on two knees. We saw from a two-on-one up grip, my opponent goes to yank away, I move in with the pull.

He goes to stand up, walks away, I move with the pull. Eventually, we're going to get opportunities to disrupt his balance, etc. Some keynote themes.

Whenever I go to grip, whatever grip I employ, whatever initial grip I employ, will always have a weak zone that my opponent can exploit. My job is to cover that weak zone with my second hand. Then, when my opponent goes to apply ever-increasing amounts of force to break the grips, my job is to go into strong structures, usually involving elbows to my own hips.

And then the third element is the idea of moving with my opponent's resistance. I don't remain stationary and turn it into a pulling match of my strength against his. Rather, I simply move with my opponent's pull.

I can dissipate the force of my opponent's rearward pull to retain an attachment to my opponent. So we've seen this critical idea of two-on-one gripping. So far, we've only been working with two-on-one wrist grips.

The moment I start to stick beyond the toe line, Bernardo Fari can easily get a hold of me and establish inside position with his foot. Like that. So when I go to approach the toe line I have to do so in a way which is relatively safe for me.

The third critical line of demarcation that I work with when I go to approach an opponent in a seated position is my opponent's centre line. If I step outside of the centre line I have given my opponent the inside position, which is going to be crucial for the leg lock game. That's a bad move on my part.

(11:30 - 11:54)
So the three lines that we work with are my opponent's toe line, which joins his big toes, the knee line, which joins his two knees, and my opponent's centre line, which will determine the battle for inside control. When I go to approach my opponent I can do so in two main ways. The first is the so-called bent over method, or a kind of defensive stance which we often refer to as a Jigotai.

(11:55 - 12:15)
In Jigotai I move towards my opponent and I match my head level with my opponent's head level. If my head is significantly higher than my opponent's it leaves my legs open to my opponent's attacks. So the first thing I do is I judge distance by staying away from the toe line, and I put my foot out wide and I match my head level with my opponent's head level.

(12:16 - 12:38)
From a situation like this there is sufficient distance from my opponent that if he wanted to move towards me it would be immediately obvious and I could play the distance game around him. He can't simply grab me, he would have to move first before he grabs me. When I go to move forward on my opponent I'm going to do so behind the safety of a thumb post.

(12:38 - 12:56)
In a thumb post I take my thumb, I turn my palm down, and I post my thumb inside my opponent's shoulder, just like so. Then I take my hand on the same side as the leg that I'm going to put forward. In my case it's going to be my right leg.

(12:57 - 13:11)
So off my thumb post I post it like so. I cross grip my training partner's wrist. Why is that important? My opponent requires at least one arm to go in for his various attacks from bottom position.

(13:12 - 13:24)
Usually the best arm he can employ is the arm that controls my forward leg. So my right leg is best controlled by his left arm. That's the usual order of operations.

(13:24 - 13:37)
If my opponent goes in with this arm, that tends to expose his back to me. That would be something of a mistake on his part. So as a general rule, if I step forward with my right leg, he's best off using his left arm to control me.

(13:38 - 13:51)
Knowing this, we can start to enforce a gripping game. As I move in towards my opponent, my first game is a distance game. Where I stay at such a distance that if Bernardo wanted to initiate contact with me, he would have to move.

(13:52 - 14:03)
And I can simply move away from him. So by using distance, I can determine who makes contact first. Is it him or me? Because I'm standing, I have a mobility advantage.

(14:03 - 14:15)
Because I'm at a distance where he has to move, I can prevent him from getting working grips on me until I want him to. So that's the first phase of gripping, is distance. The second phase is control.

(14:16 - 14:31)

(14:31 - 14:39)
That's what's good for him. I want his head over his hips. The moment I put the head over the hips, his feet become light.

(14:39 - 14:55)
And as a result, we can start to negate our opponent's legs. So when I move in on my opponent, I come in and I put a thumb post on my training partner. It's hard for my opponent to get any kind of proactive gripping going on him because he's working through the thumb post.

(14:55 - 15:06)

(15:07 - 15:15)

(15:15 - 15:31)

(15:31 - 15:45)

Okay, we're looking at the idea of grip fighting from top position. We're looking in the context of our general theory of grip fighting.

(17:13 - 17:25)
Grip fighting has to have certain elements involved. First is the idea of developing an imposing grip. We've got to have an effective grip that our opponents can't break easily and which has some kind of effect on our opponent.

(17:25 - 17:37)
The effect we're looking for first is a negative effect. That is the ability to shut down what he wants to do to me. I can't try to impose my game upon my opponent while he is actively imposing his game upon me.

(17:38 - 17:54)
So our first thing is to negate his game so that we can then go into the next step which is to impose our own game upon them. Whenever two people are trying to impose their game upon each other at the same time it rapidly turns into a dogfight. What we want is first to shut them down and then impose our own game.

(17:54 - 18:03)
That's always the general sense in which we work our gripping game. And that way we can be said to be gripping for a purpose. We're not just coming out and touching people for no reason.

(32:06 - 32:22)
Bernardo, sit down, everybody. Let's distinguish between the knee, which you're all very familiar with, just the knee, and the knee line. The knee line is the line that joins one of my knees to the other.

(32:23 - 32:39)
It's a straight line from one knee to the other. Bernardo also has a knee line. My favourite way of dividing the human body out to make the understanding of Jiu-Jitsu easier is to use ankle lines, knee lines, hip lines, shoulder lines, and centre lines.

(32:40 - 33:04)
I use those simple demarcation points to illustrate where I want to be on the human body at any given moment in a match. In any Ashigurami situation, ultimately what I'm looking to do is to take my opponent's knee and bring it inside my knee line.

(33:06 - 33:20)
Unfortunately, many people don't distinguish between the knee and the knee line. Here, you can clearly see my knee line has not captured Bernardo Faria's knee. His knee is outside my knee line.

(33:21 - 33:35)

(35:38 - 35:45)

(36:20 - 36:38)
We're looking at the second critical skill of entering into the Ashigurami leg lock game. This is the skill of distance and connection. I have to be able to get a certain measure of distance which enables me to form an effective connection on my opponent's leg and hip.

(36:39 - 36:54)
That measuring distance is my knee line relative to my opponent's knee. I always want my opponent's knee held within my knee line. The second I fail to do that, the entire Ashigurami system will fall apart.

(36:55 - 37:28)
Learning to govern that distance between you and your opponent, between his knee and your knee line, is one of the absolute most important concepts that we work with in this entire DVD series. A huge part of what we do when we get into the nuts and bolts of forming a heel on someone later in the series will be based around that crucial concept. In fact, I would go even further and say that you, as a prospective leg locker, will find that almost 90% of your failures with leg locks can be traced back to that one simple concept.

(37:29 - 37:58)
Can you keep your opponent's knee inside your knee line? That's why we're going to spend so much time on it later in this DVD. Now, whenever I go to coach someone, when they first begin the leg lock game, there's a series of drills which I give to them in their early days which massively help their development in this notion of distance and knee control. These are gravity drills.

(37:58 - 38:24)
Gravity drills are a set of four simple drills which make my athletes work against gravity to connect their body to their opponent's leg. The beauty of gravity drills is that gravity will immediately reveal any weakness in distance and connection. You simply cannot perform a gravity drill without distance and without a strong sense of connection.

(38:26 - 38:51)
Any failures on your part will immediately be revealed by the pull of gravity as you slide down your opponent's leg and fail. Let's have a look at the first and most basic gravity drill. When I say basic, I laugh when I say this because I think it's fair to say my students are among the best leg lockers in the world, but when it's time for international competition, when they're warming up in the changing room, the first thing I make them do is gravity drills.

(38:52 - 39:13)
No matter how good you get in the leg game, I still believe that gravity drills should be a regular part of your training because they reinforce the most essential habits and characteristics of good leg locking of inside position, distance and connection. Let's have a look at the first gravity drill. It's pretty basic.

(39:13 - 39:27)
I'll go with Kong style on this one. The first gravity drill, in my opinion, is the most important and the one I want you focussing on first. This is where we face our opponent and go up into the standard form of Ashigurami.

(39:29 - 39:44)
Because this form of Ashigurami is the one we use most of the time, I believe this is the best drill for you to start on when you first begin your leg lock study. We're going to have an opponent standing over us. He's going to stand in a square stance with his feet fairly wide.

(39:44 - 39:51)
He's going to have his hands away from his hips. I don't care where you put your hands. You can cross your hands like you're in prayer.

(39:51 - 40:06)

(40:07 - 40:21)
Let's think about that. Because the ankle line is directly underneath my training partner's knee line, as the demarcation point for distance. If my hips do not go past that ankle line, I will never satisfactorily go into an Ashigurami.

(40:22 - 40:36)
So here, you can clearly see my hips have failed to breach Karl Massaro's ankle line. As a result, when I try to go into the Ashigurami, I simply can't get my leg around his leg. I can't even begin to get into the Ashigurami.

(40:40 - 40:50)
The knees are directly over the ankles. So when someone stands over me, the knee line is manifested by the line of the ankles. My hips must breach that line.

(40:50 - 41:04)
So in the drill, we start off in front of our training partner with a curved spine, like so. I take a double leg position on my training partner. I use a combination of hands and feet to pull my hips past his ankle line.

(41:05 - 41:26)
Once my hips breach the ankle line, it is the easiest thing in the world for me to throw my legs up, and my lower back must rise off the mat. If my lower back does not rise off the mat to connect to my opponent's hip, you will always get a weak and pathetic looking Ashigurami. I must throw my hips off the mat.

(41:27 - 41:41)
The only way I can do that is by curving my spine. When my spine curves, I easily go up into the air, and you can clearly see my knee line has passed his knee. I have successfully locked onto my opponent.

(41:42 - 41:50)
I have no hands. The only thing holding me in place is my leg connection. The Ashigurami is the only thing holding me in place.

(41:50 - 42:17)
The pinch of my knees with my knee line above my opponent's knee makes for a perfect connection. If there were any deficiencies in my Ashigurami, gravity would soon pull me down, and my knees would slip below his knees, and you would have a feeble looking Ashigurami. So what are we learning in this basic drill? The first skill is distance.

(42:18 - 42:36)
If I fail to breach my opponent's ankle line, which, because he's standing, is essentially my opponent's knee line, I will never get to an Ashigurami position on either side. So the first reason you'll fail here, lack of distance. As a result, I simply can't get my leg around my opponent's leg.

(42:38 - 42:51)
So the first requirement is penetration. When I go in on my training partner, it's never enough just to pull with your hands. I must learn to use my feet to penetrate past the ankle line.

(42:51 - 43:03)

(0:03 - 0:23)
We're looking at the idea of distance and connection. We've seen that the measure of distance is the relationship between my opponent's knee and my knee line. I know that I'm satisfactorily connecting and managing distance when I can retain my opponent's knee inside my knee line.

(0:24 - 0:37)

(0:50 - 1:15)

This is what most people think of when they think of Ashigurami. That's why it's often referred to as a standard Ashigurami.

(1:16 - 1:35)
The phrase I use for it is Arimi Ashigurami. Arimi simply means to enter or to go into. I use this Ashigurami primarily as a means of entering into my opponent's legs and forming an initial connection from which all the other elements of the leg lock system will kick in.

(1:36 - 1:53)
Let's have a look at what Arimi Ashigurami is. If Bernardo is seated like so. Arimi Ashigurami refers to a situation where I have my opponent's knee inside my knee line.

(1:53 - 2:08)
I have an inside leg and an outside leg. My outside leg is primarily used to govern distance. I point my toes to the outside and my foot hooks here on top of my opponent's hip.

(2:09 - 2:23)
If Bernardo leans forward, he will go into the resistance of the sole of my foot. So it helps in distance management. However, do not push and extend into your opponent's ribcage.

(2:23 - 2:35)
That is not the idea. You are actually pushing him out of the Ashigurami. In fact, what I do is I engage in a movement we call backheeling.

(3:21 - 3:32)

(3:32 - 3:42)
Where my left foot goes to my opponent's right buttock. There are pros and cons to each. There are times we use one and not the other.

(32:29 - 32:43)

(32:57 - 33:13)
If I push in the wrong direction, I will never break my opponent down to the mat. I must assess the correct direction to apply my forces. And the general rule of thumb is always apply the force across or perpendicular to your opponent's hips.

(33:16 - 33:29)
Here, I have a Rimi Ashi Garami. Kama Sado goes to interfere with it and breaks it apart. I have a choice between going over wrap or scoop grip on my training partner's leg.

(33:41 - 34:04)

(34:05 - 34:14)

(34:14 - 34:27)
I bring my knees to my chest first and then I bring him in the power curve. My lower back is off the ground. My spine is in the rocking chair shape that we talked about earlier.

(34:27 - 34:42)
As a result, as it sits directly over my head, I can easily pull his knee inside my knee line. Now, I want to apply the breakdown force perpendicular to his hips. The Kama Sado stays where he is.

(34:42 - 34:51)
It's so easy. I put him straight down. The only way he can resist me is to walk on his hands.

(34:51 - 35:01)
This is where people make mistakes. They apply the direction of force into their opponent's base of support. No, always across the hips.

(35:02 - 35:34)
So I take my right knee and turn it and lace his ankles into a winning position. We're looking at the general system of leg locks. A big theme of this instructional series is the idea that you're never going to successfully apply heel hooks at the highest levels of competition if you only think of heel hooks as an isolated move.

(35:35 - 36:09)
Any isolated move can quickly be understood and countered. Success in the leg locking game comes from a holistic approach where each move is embedded in a general system that starts with the prerequisites of elements such as grip, distancing, connection, breaking your opponent to a hip, and then ultimately works its way through various forms of Ashi Garami all the way leading to a break. Remember, there are six essential skills of heel hooking.

(36:10 - 36:37)
If you experience failure at any one of those six steps, those six skills, your heel hooks will be ineffective in championship competition. Let's say, for example, you have an outstanding Ashi Garami and a very strong heel hook. That's fine, but if you at first have some kind of faulty method of establishing inside position, you're never going to get into a position to apply those Ashi Garamis and those heel hooks in the first place.

(36:37 - 36:58)
Every subsequent step of the system is built on the success of the antecedent step. You can experience failure at any one of the six steps, so you have to be equally strong at all the various six steps in the chain. As the old cliche goes, a chain is only as strong as its weakest link.

(36:58 - 37:15)
If you're weak at any one of those six skills, eventually you're going to run into failure at the highest levels. We've seen the first three steps in the general system. These were the idea of establishing grip and inside position.

(37:15 - 37:24)
That's always step number one. We can't really differentiate between grip and inside position. They go together so tightly that I identify them as one skill.

(37:24 - 37:39)
Then we saw the second step, the idea of distance and connection. Those two are so tightly intertwined that I treat them as one skill. Then we saw the third optional skill, which is breaking your opponent down to a hip.

(37:39 - 37:50)
This is only relevant in cases where you're working from underneath your opponent. If you're on top, obviously we skip that step. Now we start getting into the next three finishing steps.

(37:51 - 38:09)
The first of these is so-called heel exposure. After that, we get into the idea of negation, how to negate my opponent's defences to the various locks that we employ. Ultimately, we get down to the sixth and final step, breaking.

(38:10 - 38:59)
These three final steps are best explained and taught, not as a general system, but as part of the three subsystems which I use. Cross-Ashigurami, Straight-Ashigurami and Reverse-Ashigurami. Our teaching is going to go in a different direction now.

We're going to take the first and arguably the most important initial form of Ashigurami, the so-called Straight-Ashiguramis. We're going to show how we go through the idea of a very, very concrete set of steps that will put us into extraordinarily strong breaking positions from where we can actually submit people. So we're going now from a more theoretical approach to leg locking to a more hands-on, nuts-and-bolts practical approach.

(38:59 - 39:28)
Let's begin our study with an understanding, or a clear statement of the Straight-Ashigurami system. Sitting down. Straight-Ashigurami refers to any situation where my opponent's left leg goes over my right hip.

So my opponent's leg does not cross my centre line. It stays on one side of my body. This stands in opposition to Cross-Ashiguramis where my opponent's leg crosses over my centre line.

(39:29 - 39:43)
This position here is very, very different from this position here. They are both essential parts of the general leg lock system. But because they are so different, I treat them and teach them differently.

(39:44 - 39:55)
There's a sense in which my leg lock system is not really one system at all. It is three systems. Cross-Ashigurami, Straight-Ashigurami and Reverse-Ashigurami entwined with each other.

(39:57 - 40:18)
The sum of those three subsystems creates the general system. My approach to the Straight-Ashigurami is quite a bit different from those which came before it. As always, we start off with an Arimi-Ashigurami.

(40:18 - 40:40)
This is our primary method of entering into the Straight-Ashigurami system. We've seen that when we work on the floor, I generally favour a straight shoelace on buttocks position so that I can close a circle around my training partner's hips and legs. This forms an effective closed wedge around my opponent's hip and knee.

(40:40 - 41:02)
This securely locks my opponent's knee inside my knee line. It enables me to put body weight on my opponent's shin, which means that when Kamosara goes to move backwards, I have a strong sense of connection to my opponent. When he goes into a rotational escape, I have a strong sense of connection to my opponent.

(41:03 - 41:30)
You can see my legs alone connect me to him without the use of my hands. Because my hands are free, I can now start using my hands to go into actual locks as opposed to having to desperately hold on to my opponent's legs and prevent myself from actually finishing my opponent. What I'm about to teach you is a complete rendition of the Outside or Straight-Ashigurami game.

(41:30 - 41:49)
You're going to see that there are some significant differences from conventional approaches. One of the most important is the idea that there's no one skill involved in Heel-Hooking. Rather, there are multiple skills.

(41:49 - 42:13)
I'm going to start by showing the four most important skills which are necessary for success in the Straight-Ashigurami game. The first one we've spent quite a bit of time on, which is the idea of distance and connection. We know that the measure of distance is the position of my knee line relative to my opponent's knee.

(42:14 - 42:46)
A simple drill which we can do is to lock around our training partner's ankle. Kamasawa is going to move back and away from me. You can clearly see that his knee is outside of my knee line.

From here, I simply turn my palm and I lift and I can drag him along the mat so that now his knee is back inside my knee line. As a result, I can quickly lock up an Ashigurami that forms a good connection. The form of Ashigurami I use is a Rimming or Entering Ashigurami.

(42:47 - 42:59)
That's my primary method of forming an initial connection to my opponent's leg. A Rimming Ashigurami is an excellent form of Ashigurami for entering the legs. In my opinion, it's the single best one.

(43:00 - 43:34)
However, as good as it is for entering the legs, it's not particularly good for subsequent steps. If the first skill was entering a connection, what's the second skill here? It's to expose my opponent's heel. No matter how strong my Ashigurami is, if I can't reliably expose my opponent's heel, I'm never going to heel hook anybody.

I have to be able to expose my opponent's heel to my wrist. Only when I actually have the lock do I become dangerous to my opponent. No one has got their leg broken by an Ashigurami itself.

(43:35 - 43:56)
What breaks the leg is the combination of Ashigurami and the breaking mechanism, the heel hook. Only in unison do they break people's legs. So, once I've established the first step, the idea of distance and connection, now it's time to go on to step number two of the outside or straight Ashigurami system, which is to expose the heel.

(43:57 - 44:20)

(44:21 - 44:34)
We're both in the same Ashigurami. His foot comes over, he has something of an Rimi Ashigurami on me, but he has heel exposure. My heel is revealed to him, his heel is hidden on the floor.

(44:35 - 44:51)
Let's have a look at that again. We've seen the first step in any Ashigurami system, in this case the straight Ashigurami system, is distance and connection. Once that's been satisfied, we've got to go hunting for our opponent's heel.

(44:53 - 45:08)
To gain entry to my opponent's legs, a Rimi Ashigurami is the single best method. However, when it's time for the second task, the task of exposing my opponent's heel, a Rimi Ashigurami comes up short. In fact, it can make me quite vulnerable.

(45:08 - 45:40)
When I go down to a shoulder in this position, my inside leg becomes unweighted and as a result, Kyle can pummel his legs through, around, and look what we've got. An exposed heel. My heel is exposed to him.

I'm in more danger here than he is. An experienced opponent can simply wait for you to load your weight onto the shoulder, unload the inside leg, and now it's me who's getting finished, not him. He has my heel exposed.

(45:41 - 45:46)
I don't have his exposed. He can heel hook me. I can't heel hook him until I get to his heel.

(45:47 - 46:26)
That's not the kind of game you want to get into. We're touching on a very important theme here. The idea that as the task I'm trying to accomplish changes, my Ashigurami must also change.

I need more than one Ashigurami to get the job done. There's four distinct tasks that we have to fulfil if we're going to apply a successful heel hook here, and I need four different Ashiguramis one for each of those respective tasks. If I try to use a one size fits all, one Ashigurami to perform all four tasks, I will always run into problems.

(46:27 - 46:54)

(46:55 - 47:05)

(47:05 - 47:17)

(47:17 - 47:45)

As a result, I can easily get an initial grip on my training partner. For the purposes of the initial grip, I recommend the use of what we call a false grip. That's where my wrist bends 90 degrees like a gymnast on the Olympic rings who bends his wrist 90 degrees for a stronger grip.

(47:47 - 48:22)
This is not designed for breaking. It's just designed for control. It connects my opponent's shoelaces to my ribs.

(48:23 - 48:51)

(49:23 - 49:43)
We're starting to go through the nuts and bolts of the straight Ashi Garami system. The best way to understand it is in terms of four distinct steps. The big thing that we're trying to push is that each distinct step or skill requires a different form of Ashi Garami.

(49:44 - 50:00)
For many years, people tended to make no distinction between the Ashi Garami and the lock itself. A big part of my system is to strongly distinguish between those two elements. The Ashi Garami controls my opponent's legs and hips.

(50:01 - 50:27)
The heel hook, or whichever mechanism, the Achilles lock, the figure four toehold, is the actual breaking mechanism. What I'm trying to describe to you is the idea that there are four distinct skills in any successfully applied heel hook in a straight Ashi Garami situation. And we need a different form of Ashi Garami to deal with each one of those distinct skills.

(50:27 - 51:04)
Any given Ashi Garami has good points, bad points, strengths, weaknesses. We need to be able to exploit the strengths of a given Ashi Garami in one area and avoid the weaknesses. So, for example, a Rumi Ashi Garami is extremely good for entering into my opponent's legs.

It's fast, it's quick, it gets quick access and quick control of an opponent's leg and hip. However, it's not very good for exposing my opponent's heel. Much better for that purpose is an inside Ashi Garami, where my leg passes the centre line and exposes my opponent's heel to my wrist so I can start the heel hook game.

(51:04 - 51:39)

We've seen that after that, we need to expose our opponent's heel.

(51:39 - 52:00)

(52:00 - 52:18)
The number one forms of escape all involve turning in the direction of the lock. If I stay with an inside Ashi Garami, there's a sense in which I'm applying force with my Ashi Garami in the same direction as my opponent's escape. That's not a good thing.

(52:19 - 52:36)
I always want to oppose the direction of my opponent's escape with my Ashi Garami. My opponent's escapes are based around three primary methods of overcoming my attempts to attack the leg. The first is rotation.

(52:36 - 53:07)
If we go back, the first method of relieving pressure and ultimately escaping is for my opponent to rotate with me. So, if I'm trying to apply breaking force and then I roll, and roll, and roll, I can't apply finalising pressure on my opponent's leg, because the direction of his roll is the same direction as my breaking pressure. Every time I go to apply breaking pressure, he's negating it by rolling in the same direction that I'm applying pressure.

(53:07 - 53:22)
So, we never get through to a break until we go out of balance or what have you. So, that's one way in which you can negate my attempts at a heel hook. A second way is to apply the principle of distance.

(53:24 - 53:51)
If Banara, once I get a hold of his heel, can come up to his knees and put a foot on my buttocks and then push, his knee will come out from my knee line. And as a result, there's so much distance between his knee and my knee line, I simply lose control of the leg. Even if my opponent just moved a matter of inches, he can defeat my lock.

(53:51 - 54:06)
Let's face this way. If I lock up here, you can clearly see Banara's knee is inside my knee line. If he moves even a matter of inches, he is now frustrated with my attempt at a heel hook.

(54:06 - 54:28)
The moment my opponent's knee slips inside to this position, I've lost the heel hook. No amount of pressure now will have any effect. (54:28 - 54:48)
Now, the heel hook is doomed to failure. He can either rotate with me to alleviate stress on the knee, or most importantly, play the distance game, where he can do a partial rotation and then free his knee. The next method, of course, is to interfere with the Ashigurami itself.

(54:48 - 55:38)
He can push the Ashigurami leg down and then from here, even though I have the heel, I no longer have a true Ashigurami pressure. Now I can easily turn in either direction to escape the lock. So my opponent has three primary methods of shutting down my attempts at a heel hook.

He can either use rotational methods, where he spins either at the same speed as me, or even better, faster than I'm turning. He can use distance methods, where usually in combination with rotation, he will pull his knee free and escape from my knee line. Or he can just break the structure of my Ashigurami.

Those are the three methods by which he's going to defeat my heel hook. So what I need is an ability to shut down those three defensive elements. I'm going to show you how we do that by switching to a new form of Ashigurami.

(55:40 - 55:53)
Okay, I have an Arumi Ashigurami on Banada. That's good for connection. We saw that when we switched to an inside Ashigurami, like so, we now get a heel exposure.

(57:23 - 57:34)

(57:35 - 57:51)

(57:52 - 58:10)
If we go back, all the problems here come from situations where my knee remains static in his turns. When he turns, and my knee remains static, that's exactly when he slips out. What I want in these situations, is my knee following his knee.

(58:11 - 58:23)
There is a certain arc through which my opponent rotates. Within that arc, I can break my opponent's leg. If he goes beyond that arc, I can no longer do so.

(58:23 - 58:46)
If I remain static, and my opponent turns, he's gone past the arc of breaking. So what I want to do in these situations, once I lock up and apply pressure, is to get my opponent to turn, and my knee follows his knee. As he turns and runs, I point my knee at the floor.

(58:47 - 59:02)

If my legs are open, Bernardo can push... Bernardo can push my foot and break the structure of the Ashigurami.

(1:00:31 - 1:00:46)

(1:02:18 - 1:02:36)
This far hip Ashi is a very, very useful way of locking our hips to our opponent's hips. In any situation where we're locked in in that fashion, my opponent has only three avenues of escape. He's either got to separate my legs, break the Ashigurami.

(1:02:36 - 1:02:47)
That's impossible because I've got a triangle locked around his hips. He's got to rotate faster than me. That's impossible because I simply point my knee at the floor and that guarantees we rotate at the same speed.

(1:02:48 - 1:03:23)

That's going to put us into the fourth and final step. This is the step of breaking our opponent. And from here, we use a very unique form of Ashigurami, the so-called Outside Ashigurami, where both of my feet end up on the outside of my opponent's legs.

(1:03:25 - 1:03:52)
This is going to bring in perhaps the single most important theme of the entire leg lock system. The idea that when it's time to go to break an opponent's legs, it is absolutely crucial that my Ashigurami go in the opposite direction of the actual break. Let's look at why so many people failed with heel hooks for so many years.

(1:03:55 - 1:04:35)
Most people make no distinction between the force of the breaking mechanism and the force of the Ashigurami. If you learned nothing else from this video, learn this. When it's time to break someone's leg, the force of your Ashigurami must oppose the force of your break.

You must distinguish between the mechanism of control, Ashigurami, and the mechanism of breaking, the heel hook. For generations, people never distinguished between the two. And so you would see many experts teaching an Ashigurami that went in a given direction, and a heel hook that went in the same direction.

(1:04:36 - 1:04:55)
Because of that, there was no reason why the opponent couldn't simply spin out of the lock. That was easy. Or, even worse, you would go to apply pressure on the heel hook, but because your Ashigurami and the heel hook went in the same direction, your Ashigurami more or less pushed him out of the heel hook.

(1:04:55 - 1:05:40)
And so there was inadequate pressure to actually get successful results. Because of this, people had to apply more and more force on the heel hook to get any form of result. So you would see people violently gripping across the chest with results that tended to either result in no break at all, which is an ineffective lock, or a catastrophic break because it was applied without control in the gym.

And so heel hooks got a terrible reputation as a move which either wasn't very effective, or tended to result in bad breaks amongst the beginners. What we want to do is to do the exact opposite of this. We never want our Ashigurami going in the same direction as the force of a lock.

(1:05:40 - 1:05:58)
How do I know which direction the force of the Ashigurami is going? Well, I can look across a room and tell you exactly where it's going. It goes in the direction that the knees are pointing. Wherever my knees are pointing tells me which direction the force of the Ashigurami is going.

(1:05:59 - 1:06:24)
So if my knees point to the inside, that's the same direction as the heel hook. For a right-handed heel hook, everything is going from right to left. But so is my Ashigurami.

That's going from right to left. So Bernardo finds it very easy to spin and escape. Okay? What I want to do is to create a situation where my knees point in the opposite direction of the heel hook.

(1:06:25 - 1:06:37)
And the single best, tightest method of doing this, it's not the only one, but it's in my opinion the best, is the outside Ashigurami. Why is it the best? Because it's a reinforced wedge. My feet are locked.

(1:06:38 - 1:06:51)
I could apply an Ashigurami with my feet pointing to the outside with an Urimi Ashigurami. It can be done. And if your opponents are not of a high skill level, I'll often do this.

(1:06:51 - 1:07:03)
You'll often see myself and my students apply a heel hook just from here. It's usually because the opponent is not very knowledgeable about heel hooks. And if I feel that, then yes, absolutely, I can break them right here.

(1:07:03 - 1:07:33)
But you'll notice my knees are pointing to the outside. My knees always betray the direction of force of the Ashigurami. Here, clearly, the knees are pointing to the outside.

So as I apply the heel hook, even one-handed, you can see the heel hook is going over my left shoulder, but my Ashigurami is going out to my right-hand side. Okay? And so I can quickly create tension in the leg. Okay? But this is an unreinforced wedge.

(1:07:34 - 1:07:51)
So my opponent can easily push the foot down and cause problems for me. The beauty of an outside Ashigurami is that whether my feet are locked top over bottom or bottom over top, here, my feet reinforce each other. So when Banara goes to push my feet apart, it's shockingly difficult.

(1:07:51 - 1:08:04)
Okay? And then from here, my knees are pointing to the outside. Even a minor lift on the heel will create breaking pressure. Okay? Now, we've seen the critical four steps.

(1:08:05 - 1:08:13)
Entry and connection is number one. We use a Rimi Ashigurami as the method of controlling the legs. Heel exposure is step number two.

(1:08:13 - 1:08:29)
We've seen inside Ashigurami is the primary method of doing this. The third step is negation of our opponent's attempts at escape. Far hip Ashigurami is our favourite method of preventing the three forms of escape.

(1:08:30 - 1:09:02)
Rotation, distance, and breaking the Ashigurami. And now we're going into the fourth and final phase of heel hooking, breaking. And we've seen our favourite method of doing this is from the so-called outside Ashigurami.

Let's have a look at how the whole sequence was tied together in four simple, easy to understand steps that can be done in a matter of seconds on a strongly resisting opponent. We start off with an Arimi Ashigurami. Just like so.

(1:09:03 - 1:09:42)
So when Banada goes to pull away from me, it's very, very difficult. We get to the heel very quickly. Okay? Once we have the heel from the inside Ashigurami, okay, we make a switch to far hip and lock up.

When Banada tries to get away from me, I point my knees down to the floor. He goes pull, pull, pull. It's very, very difficult.

I lock up the heel hook and I turn and create pressure. Okay? Now we have the outside Ashigurami in place. I make sure that my two legs back heel, pulling back, never pushing with my legs, always pulling.

(1:09:42 - 1:09:50)
My top knee comes over the top and we get a strong breaking position with the outside Ashigurami heel hook.


## Volume 4

(0:04 - 0:16)
We saw earlier that there are distinct steps in this system. Okay, all the steps are arranged sequentially. If I can't establish inside position, the whole system stops right there.

(0:16 - 0:32)
Okay, so everything begins with inside position. Once we establish inside position, then it's all about creating a sense of distance and connection. I have to be close enough to capture my opponent's knee within my knee line and form a solid connection to my opponent's hips and legs.

(0:33 - 0:53)
In the case of the straight Ashigurami game, we've seen that that form of initial connection is typically an Arimi Ashigurami or entering Ashigurami. That was a great way to form a solid connection to our opponent's hips and legs. Then we went to phase two of that particular subsystem.

(0:54 - 1:41)
The idea of creating heel exposure. If I can't expose my opponent's heel, nothing else counts. I don't care how good you're Ashigurami, I don't care how strong you are, I don't care how tight your heel looks.

If you can't physically expose your opponent's heel, nothing else is going to follow on. We've seen inside Ashiguramis have the best method of exposing our opponent's heel in this situation. Then, once you've exposed your opponent's heel, in competition, that's when the panic buttons start lighting up in your opponent.

They will do everything possible to get out of that situation. You will be absolutely stunned at the amount of resistance you will receive at world championship level from talented athletes who know how to get out of these positions, who have a good understanding of Jiu-Jitsu and good understanding of Legos. The amount of resistance you have to deal with is staggering.

(1:42 - 1:54)
You need every possible mechanism to shut that resistance down. We've seen that resistance comes in three forms. Your opponent can either break up your Ashigurami, destroying the integrity of the Ashigurami itself.

(1:54 - 2:12)
They can start rotating in a way where they're rotating faster than you're rotating and shut down your ability to apply force to the heel. Or, they can play a distance game and pull their knee out from the knee line. In practise, usually it's a combination of all three which is used to escape from a heel hook.

(2:13 - 2:47)
We saw a four-step system to shut this down. We formed a connection to our opponent's leg, we exposed the heel, we shut down their escape attempts and now we got into a breaking position. We've seen that when it comes time to break, we favour more than or any other in the straight Ashigurami subsystem.

The idea of an outside Ashigurami finish. The key to an outside Ashigurami is to understand that we get opposing forces. My Ashigurami goes in one direction and my mechanism of breaking the heel hook goes in another.

(2:48 - 3:00)
Think about this like tearing a piece of paper. If I put a piece of paper in your hand and ask you to tear it, it's the easiest thing in the world. Why? Because when you go to rip the paper, your hands go in opposing directions.

(3:02 - 3:19)
Breaking someone's leg or tearing someone's leg is no different from tearing a piece of paper. Ultimately, it's about putting forces in opposing directions to get the job done. The problem most people have is they apply breaking forces in one direction.

(3:19 - 3:36)
Tragically, they usually do it in the same direction that their Ashigurami is going. So, not only do they have a weak breaking force, but they're actually assisting their opponent in escaping by pushing them in the exact direction of their escape. It's a disaster and that's why heel hooks were so ineffective for so long.

(3:36 - 4:06)
What I did is to change the way people finished heel hooks in the Ashigurami positions by opposing the force of the Ashigurami directly against their opponent's escape and against the direction of the break. I strongly differentiated between the Ashigurami, which goes in one direction, and the break, which goes in the opposite direction. In this way, you can create strongly opposed forces on the leg that will tear a man's leg just as easily as you tear a piece of paper.

(4:07 - 4:40)
We need to go now into the precise elements of breaking mechanics. I'm a huge believer in the idea of coaching breaking mechanics to a point where you ultimately come to believe that if you can get into a breaking position on anybody in the world, you can break their leg with the greatest of ease. If you don't believe you have this power in top level competition, you won't fight hard to get to your opponent's legs.

(4:41 - 4:50)
I don't care how smooth your Ashigurami entries are. I don't care how good you are at exposing your opponent's ankle. I don't care how good you are at negating their escapes.

(4:50 - 5:19)
If you don't believe you have the capacity to break your opponent's leg at will, you will not try hard under duress and pressure and competition to get through to an Ashigurami and get to the break. Your own mind will find ways to prevent you from getting there. But if you believe in your heart of hearts that you have the ability to break a man's leg with a well-applied heel hook under the stress of competition, you will find ways to get to that breaking position in the heat of combat.

(5:19 - 5:39)
Let's have a look at some of the fine details of breaking from an outside Ashigurami situation. We know the big picture. The big picture is I want my Ashigurami going in one direction and my breaking mechanism, the heel hook, going in the opposite direction, just like tearing a piece of paper.

(5:39 - 6:00)
We know already that the force of my Ashigurami is always revealed by the direction my knees are pointing. Wherever my knees point, that's where the force, the direction of force is being applied in the Ashigurami. We know that typically outside Ashigurami is used as part of the four-step sequence.

(6:00 - 6:18)
We start with a Rimi Ashigurami, we go into Inside Ashigurami, we go across into Far Hip Ashigurami, and now ultimately we end up in the Outside Ashigurami to finish our opponent. When I go into Outside Ashigurami, there are sub-varieties of Ashigurami. I can just have my feet close together.

(6:18 - 6:25)
I can post my foot on my training partner's hip. I can have bottom leg under. I can have bottom leg over.

(6:26 - 6:37)
They're all good in terms of breaking power. Some are slightly safer than others. I usually play between all of these minor variations and I've had good results with all of them.

(6:37 - 6:56)
Which one you choose ultimately will be a personal choice. Now, when it's time to go in and break my training partner's leg, I want to maximise my use of lever and fulcrum. So often I see people failing to do this by gripping improperly.

(6:57 - 7:14)
So often I see people, when they go to put a grip in, putting their wrist in a mechanically disadvantageous position. Most people are happy simply to lock their hands and just work for the heel hook from this position. Don't be one of those people.

(7:14 - 7:34)
There's a clear demarcation line which signals whether or not I'm working mechanically and in a mechanically efficient way with lever and fulcrum here. That line is your opponent's ankle. When I look at my opponent's ankle, I want to take my wrist and ensure that my wrist operates below my opponent's ankle.

(7:35 - 8:02)
The moment my wrist starts to go above my opponent's ankle, it's very easy for my opponent to point his toes and slip his heel inside my wrist. This is a constant source of trouble. In addition, even if I go to apply breaking pressure now, I'm doing so in a way where I cannot employ simultaneously an efficient pushing and pulling force on my opponent's leg.

(8:03 - 8:22)
I've said a number of times now, I must work with opposing forces when it comes time to break my opponent's leg. It's important you understand there are two opposing forces, not one. The first opposing force is between the heel hook and the ashigurami.

(8:22 - 8:38)
That's the first, but there's a second one. That's the distinction between the push on the toes and the pull on the heel. Ultimately, what I want to do is to create a pull on the heel and a simultaneous push on the toes.

(8:39 - 8:52)
If I am not doing that, I am never going to be able to maximise my breaking pressure with a heel hook. So there are two ends to the lever of the foot, not one. The first end is the line of the toes.

(8:53 - 9:07)
That's this line that goes from the first knuckle of the big toe to the first knuckle of the smallest toe. I need to take my opponent's toes and put them into my bicep. Not the armpit, the bicep.

(9:09 - 9:29)
I need to line my forearm up with my opponent's foot. The more my foot, sorry, the more my forearm lines up with my opponent's shin, the less successful I will be. I want my forearm to line up with the foot here.

(9:30 - 9:48)
Never with the shin. When my forearm lines up with the shin, a simple pointing of his toes and a heel slip, he'll escape every time. I put the toes in the bicep and I line everything up with my wrist below his ankle with the foot.

(9:49 - 10:06)
When I go to lock, I have several alternatives. The most versatile is a palm-to-palm lock. Off the palm-to-palm lock, I get an ability to use my forearm as a lever arm on my opponent's shin, like so.

(10:07 - 10:24)
This is one useful form of lock. Another very useful form of lock in this situation is for me to cross my wrists, like so. This has the advantage of reducing the circumference of the grip around my opponent's heel and creating strong pressure.

(10:24 - 10:32)
