I can go further with this and lock figures of four. I can go still further and lock reverse figures of four. They're all good.

(10:32 - 10:43)
I'll be spending some time on this very shortly. But our first thing is to make sure that our forearm lines up with our training partner's foot. That's always our number one concern.

(10:44 - 11:04)
In order to maximise my use of lever and fulcrum in this situation, I have to understand two ends of the lever. Toe, toe line, and heel below the ankle. So that when I go to lock up with my training partner and my feet come in, I can go into a strong finishing position.

(11:04 - 11:12)
When I go to finish, I need to do two things. I need to push on the toes and pull on the heel. So we get opposing forces.

(11:13 - 11:33)
The only way I can do this is to start by turning my right elbow back into my ribs. Then by coming up on my right elbow and lifting, I generate extreme breaking pressure. So, when I lock in, I don't want to be down on my shoulder.

(11:34 - 11:45)
I want to do my finishes on my elbow. The first thing is I connect my elbow to my ribs. That's going to create a very strong pushing force on the toe line.

(11:47 - 12:00)
Then I want to lift on the heel so that my head rises in a well-applied heel hook. There's a critical rule which you must understand. The rule of head and hands.

(12:01 - 12:22)
If my head, as I apply the heel hook, moves away from my hands, power will bleed out of the heel hook. Wherever my head goes, my hands must follow so that everything moves and the distance between my head and hands never changes. I'll say this again.

(12:22 - 12:39)
If the head moves and the hands don't, all of the power will bleed rapidly out of the heel hook. I must form, contour my body over his, and then head and hands move together. With the head moving in a curve away and up.

(12:39 - 12:49)
The head and hands must move in a curve away from the mat. In this way, you can create ungodly breaking pressure. So, our drill.

(12:50 - 13:06)
We put our fist inside and we put the toes inside our bicep. We align our forearm with our training partner's foot. We make sure our wrist is below the ankle, never above the ankle.

(13:09 - 13:23)
I'm going to demonstrate is a palm to palm grip. I cover my training partner's shoelaces with my chest. I employ a back heel that makes sure my ashi garami goes in one direction and the heel hook goes in another.

(13:23 - 13:55)
Now, I begin everything with a push on the toes by connecting my elbows to my ribs and then a pull on the heel where my head and hands never change distance between each other. I'll just demonstrate one time. Okay, once again, we're in the outside ashi garami position and I want to go in on my opponent's leg.

(13:55 - 14:11)
I need to take my opponent's toe line to my bicep. So, I lift and I put the toes into the bicep. The next thing is I put my wrist below his ankle, just like so.

(14:12 - 14:25)
Then from here, when we lock up, we're going to start with a palm to palm grip. I want to use my elbow on top of the shin to lock everything in place. My feet pull inwards.

(14:25 - 14:34)
I connect my elbow in towards my own ribs. I haven't even begun the lock yet. I've only just put tension in the leg.

(14:35 - 14:46)
From here, we start the action of lifting the heel and we'll get the break. That's tough. Jesus, that was strong.

(14:50 - 15:26)
We're starting now to get into the fourth and final phase of one of the most important subsystems of the overall general leg lock system, the straight ashi garami subsystem. We're looking at the idea of the breaking phase, which is the combination of a series of steps. It began with entering and connecting to our opponent's legs and hips, then creating heel exposure, then the idea of negating our opponent's attempts at escape, and then finally finishing our opponent with a strong break.

(15:26 - 15:44)
I want to spend some time now on a general theory of breaking in jiu-jitsu. Jiu-jitsu, of course, is based around the idea of ultimately controlling people until you can either break a limb or strangle them. It's very, very important we have a clear understanding of what we should be trying to do any time we go in to break someone's joints.

(15:45 - 16:04)
There are three critical elements behind the general theory of breaking. The first we've already alluded to. This is the idea that whenever we go to tear someone's joints, knees, arms, or what have you, it's very, very important that we work with forces in opposing directions.

(16:04 - 16:22)
There is a direct analogy between tearing pieces of paper and tearing knees. You have to be able to apply forces upon your opponent's knees and ankles in opposing directions. As a general rule, I want my ashi-gurami going in one direction and my breaking mechanism, the heel hook, going in another.

(16:23 - 16:38)
Now, a second key element to the idea of breaking. The notion of follow-through. It is absolutely essential that you be able to take an opponent up to the point of breaking and then go beyond it.

(16:39 - 16:55)
Just as a good boxer never throws a punch at his opponent, but rather through his opponent, so too will submission holds. All the time I see talented athletes who can get to a breaking point. I'm not interested in you getting to a breaking point.

(16:55 - 17:03)
I'm interested in how you're going to go past the breaking point. This is the notion of follow-through. Think about a golfer hitting a golf ball.

(17:04 - 17:26)
Imagine we had a golf ball set up on a tee and we went to swing as hard as we could, but the second we made contact, we stopped the club. How far do you think that golf ball would travel? Not very far at all. Compare that with a golfer who not only makes connection with the ball, but follows through all the way and sends it hundreds of metres down the fairway.

(17:27 - 17:46)
When you go to break the human joints, you must do the same thing. You must be able to get to the breaking point and still have sufficient drive to be able to go far beyond it and through. Let's have a look at a situation where working from outside Ashigurami, we see exactly how this applies.

(17:47 - 18:15)
We've seen that once we get in on our training partner's legs and put ourselves in a breaking position, there's a certain point where we put tension into our training partner's legs. When I start the action of pushing on my training partner's toes and pulling on the heel, there comes a certain point where we're getting close to the break. I'm going to take Bernardo close to a point where there's some tension in the leg.

(18:15 - 18:26)
I'll just stop there. Now, you can clearly see my body is not anything close to being fully extended. This is where I am, and this is where Bernardo will begin tapping.

(18:27 - 18:49)
But you can clearly see that if I remove Bernardo's foot, from here, I still have a tremendous movement potential far beyond the point of tapping. I always have to arrange things so that when I get to the breaking point, I have a capacity to continue going on and through. If I don't have that, I'm not going to be able to break people's legs.

(18:49 - 19:00)
Yes, you'll put people under stress. Yes, you'll make them uncomfortable. But at world championship level, there has to be a viable threat of a genuine break if people are going to tap.

(19:01 - 19:22)
You will be stunned at how much pain, pressure, and torment people are prepared to deal with in pursuit of gold medals and money. In order to make people tap at that level, there has to be a strong sense of follow through. So once again, I can never have my body at full extension by the time I arrive at a tapping point.

(19:22 - 19:50)
There has to be some degree where when I walk into my opponent here, Bernardo is ready to tap here, but I still have an ability to follow through on my training partner. How do we train this ability to develop follow through? My favourite method of training this is the one-handed drill. This is where I perform a heel hook on my training partner with only one hand.

(19:51 - 20:15)
When we're in competition, of course, we want to lock our hands together to maximise our breaking ability. But in training, what I will often have students do is work from a situation where they lock up with one hand, just like so. From here, they apply the ashi garami with their legs, and with one hand, they practise the skill of turning the corner and then forming a lift on the heel.

(20:16 - 20:41)
Once you get to a position like this, there's going to come a point where just the action of your forearm muscles can generate a tap in an opponent. My hands are not even locked, and yet my opponent is in a position where they can't really tolerate much more. From here, I can generate tremendous follow through pressure, which will go through my opponent's leg.

(20:42 - 20:57)
I'll say this again because it's so important. It's not enough to get to a breaking point. You have to train yourself to get to a breaking point and still have sufficient follow through to go through and get a decisive break should the situation demand it.

(21:02 - 21:16)
We're looking at a general theory of breaking. We've seen already two key components. The first is this idea that we want forces in opposing directions upon the joint that we're attacking.

(21:16 - 21:27)
Until you have opposing directions, you'll never generate a genuine ripping force. Think always of the analogy of a piece of paper. Two hands moving in opposite directions makes it such an easy task.

(21:29 - 21:42)
Second was the idea of follow through. The idea here was that any time we're engaged in athletic activity, we have to project forces upon our opponent. Whether it be with a punch, a throw, or a strike upon something with a club.

(21:43 - 21:55)
We have to be able to follow through the object upon which we're imposing that force. You can't just go to the object and stop. You have to go through the object in order to actually do damage.

(21:56 - 22:13)
Joint breaking is exactly the same. I have to be able to arrange my body in such a way that I get to the breaking point and I still have a strong potential to continue in the direction of the break in order to fully break the limb. Now, the third key element in our theory of breaking.

(22:13 - 22:29)
The idea of creating tension and taking the slack out of the joint prior to breaking. Think about an analogy. If I hang a rope from the ceiling and the rope is very long and coils around the floor so that the rope is very slack.

(22:30 - 22:41)
If I give you a sharp knife and ask you to cut that rope, you will have to whip your hand at tremendous speed in order to be able to go through the rope. Why? Because the rope is slack. There's no tension in the rope.

(22:42 - 23:01)
So the only way to go through it is through speed. If I take that same rope and I stretch it from the ceiling tight down to the floor with a heavy weight so that it's tight and vibrating like a piano wire. Now, when you take that same knife, it's the easiest thing in the world to cut it with just a single blow.

(23:01 - 23:23)
Why? Because there's tension in the rope and as a result the cut will have a catastrophic effect upon the rope. Human joints are exactly the same. If my opponent's joint is in a relaxed state of being where there's no tension whatsoever upon the ligaments and tendons, it's very hard to break the joints.

(23:24 - 23:48)
But if I first create conditions where my opponent's tendons and ligaments are vibrating with tension and tightness, then the act of breaking is extremely easy. Let's have a look at a demonstration. Whenever I get into a breaking position, it's up to me to create a sense of tension and slack removal before I begin the actual break.

(23:48 - 24:08)
So when I come into Kawasara's leg and lock up and I get everything in place, my first thing is to maximise tension in the ligaments and tendons in the knee and ankle before I even begin the action of breaking. This is done in several distinct steps, the most important of which is my Ashigurami. My legs backheel.

(24:08 - 24:23)
Again, backheeling refers to the action of my heels coming back to my own buttocks, just like so. From here, I want to start positioning my head. I bring my head around, lined up, so that my body goes into a straight line.

(24:23 - 24:30)
I don't want my body bent, like so. That relieves tension on my training partner. So the first action is the backheel of my legs.

(24:31 - 24:48)
Then my head goes around so my body starts to go into a straight line. Then we connect both of our elbows close to our ribcage. As the elbows come in close to the ribcage, the head and the hands move together so that we feel extreme tension coming into the leg.

(24:48 - 25:04)
I can feel at this point that Carl Massaro's tendons and ligaments are starting to vibrate. At that point, even a small amount of additional pressure will create the break that I'm looking for. What I don't want is to attack my opponent's heel in a state of relaxation.

(25:05 - 25:19)
You often see people make this mistake. There's no tension whatsoever in either my legs or his, and then they try to use speed to jump into the lock. It's a hit or miss policy where most of the time it misses and occasionally when it hits it's an uncontrolled break.

(25:20 - 25:33)
A much better way for us to operate is always to lock everything up and get that tension running through my training partner's body. You will see this in your opponent's musculature. The actual legs start to tighten up.

(25:33 - 25:46)
The muscles around the legs start to go into a protective mode where they tighten up around my opponent's body. You'll feel that tension through your opponent's body. Now, with maximum tension, the break becomes so easy.

(25:46 - 26:07)
The result is that you can break people in minimal distance, and that's what you're looking for. If I have to move my opponent's body or my own body a significant distance to get a break, that means the chances of success in international competition are relatively low. The object is to be able to minimise the distance in which I can affect the break.

(26:07 - 26:30)
This is done by transmitting tremendous tension throughout the leg first prior to the break so that the break itself comes from just a tiny movement. It's very, very difficult for your opponent to stop those small movements. As we lock up, you can see from here I start the tension at the legs.

(26:30 - 26:52)
The legs pull me into my opponent, we tie everything up, and now in a matter of millimetres I can get a break. As opposed to a situation where there's no real tension in the legs and I have to move significant distances to get any effect at all. This is the third keynote theme in our theory of breaking.

(26:53 - 27:26)
We've seen the ideas of opposing forces, the idea of follow-through, and now the idea of taking the slack out of the joint first prior to the break. If we can reliably perform these three behaviours prior to actually trying to break the joint, you're going to have a lot of success and put your opponents under a lot of pressure with your heel hooking. We've been looking at the notion of breaking mechanics for heel hooking.

(27:27 - 27:47)
One of the most important components of a strong breaking heel hook is the nature in which I grip my opponent's legs. We've seen different Ashiguramis, we've seen how we go to exert force at the end of a lever. Let's look now at the role that different grips play when we go to attack our opponent's legs.

(27:48 - 28:01)
First, a little backdrop on gripping. I can grip my hands in various different kinds of ways, and as we progress with different grips you'll notice something interesting starts to happen. When I lock my hands together there's obviously different ways I can do so.

(28:01 - 28:18)
The first is generally a ten-finger method where my ten fingers lock up with each other like so. When I lock a ten-finger method you'll see there's a certain circumference around my arms, the circle of my arms. As I go through different grips that circumference gets smaller.

(28:19 - 28:33)
When I change from ten-finger to palm-to-palm, there's a small decrease in the circumference of my arms. As I go to a wrist-to-wrist grip that circumference gets even smaller. When I go to a figure-four grip it goes even smaller still.

(28:34 - 29:01)
So, in the case of strangulation for example, if I start off with a certain grip like so, the wide circumference makes for a rather inefficient strangling grip. As I tighten things up, by going to a palm-to-palm grip, we get a tighter circumference around the neck and a tighter strangle. And so ultimately, by the time we use figure-four methods, we have a very tight grip around the neck and as a result, very strong strangulation.

(29:02 - 29:18)
So, as we progress from ten-finger to palm-to-palm, to wrist-to-wrist, to figure-four grips, the circumference of our arms decreases. We can use this in the case of heel hooks. Very often, I will start with a palm-to-palm grip.

(29:19 - 29:29)
In my opinion, this is the most versatile form of gripping that I work with. And it has certain advantages to it that we'll soon see. But this is not the only way of working.

(29:29 - 29:50)
And we've seen that as we progress through various grips, leading all the way up to figure-four, we're decreasing the circumference of our arms around our training partner's ankle, you can get additional tightness. And myself and my students will often employ these methods to create stronger heel hooks in certain scenarios. Let's have a look at the pros and cons of each different kind of grip.

(29:51 - 30:05)
Working from an outside-Ashi-Gurami situation, since that's our favourite breaking grip, we go to work like so. Let's look first at the palm-to-palm grip. The advantage of palm-to-palm is that it's very versatile, and it does have one very good mechanical feature.

(30:06 - 30:49)
When I come up and lock onto my training partner's ankle, always working below the heel, when I lock my palm-to-palm grip, the wide circumference that it generates enables me to take my elbow and employ that top elbow as a wedge on top of my training partner's shin. If I have my elbow outside my opponent's shin, there's a lot of slack around my opponent's heel, and quite easy for my opponent to start escaping. But when I lock up on my training partner and I put my elbow on top of the heel like so, it tightens everything up, and it creates a situation where my two elbows come in very close to each other, and now from here we get tremendous breaking force.

(30:52 - 31:08)
Nonetheless, there's times, for whatever reason, you feel you cannot get your elbow on top of the shin. And now the palm-to-palm grip will tend to be relatively ineffective. It might be because my opponent is posting his hand inside my tricep, and as a result I can't get my elbow in the desired position.

(31:09 - 31:27)
In these situations, I will switch grips, and I will go to a wrist-to-wrist grip, like so. Once I employ a wrist-to-wrist grip, I can no longer get my elbow on top of my shin. But I don't need to, because the circumference of my arm grip is decreased, so I get tightness in other ways.

(31:27 - 31:50)
Now, I lock my elbow outside my opponent's shin, instead of on top. As Kamosara goes to hold me off in situations like this, it's extremely easy for me to generate bone-breaking force in this, or ligament tearing force, in this position. So if I feel I cannot get my desired elbow position, a nice alternative for me is to lock in a wrist-to-wrist grip on my training partner.

(31:51 - 32:05)
Now, we get very, very strong breaking mechanics. This can become even stronger, or even tighter, when I lock up a figure-four grip on my training partner's ankle, like so. Now, when he goes to move away, we get tremendous breaking pressure.

(32:05 - 32:24)
This has an additional benefit. It puts my chest as a wedge on top of my training partner's shoelaces, and it almost precludes the idea of hand-fighting. When my opponent goes to separate my hands, there's just nothing to grab, and as a result, we quickly get tremendous breaking pressure, regardless of our orientation.

(32:25 - 32:37)
Now, the last, and arguably the strongest, of all these grips is the reverse figure-four grip. I start off like so. My opponent is interested in stopping my hands from locking.

(32:37 - 32:57)
So I shake my arms out, I pass my arms around the corner, and I put what appears to be the wrong wrist inside and underneath my opponent's heel. Now, I get a unique ability to be able to push my elbow in towards my training partner's hips. At the same time, I can pull on the heel.

(32:58 - 33:31)
This grip is so strong that even from the weakest forms of ashi-garami break, even when my opponent is turned over, from here, you can still get tremendous breaking pressure by pointing your elbow at your training partner's buttocks and lifting on the heel. We will often make use of these various grips when we're going into the breaking phase. Once I get my initial grip in place, I see the resistance I'm getting and I assess whether I'm going to go palm-to-palm, which leaves some space for my opponent to hand-fight.

(33:31 - 33:44)
He can separate my hands here. Wrist-to-wrist, which greatly closes the space and makes hand-fighting much more difficult. Figure-four, which almost completely closes hand-space and makes hand-fighting almost impossible.

(33:45 - 34:18)
And reverse figure-four, which is ridiculously strong to the point where if my opponent goes to move out, twist down, etc., regardless of our orientation, we're going to be getting very, very strong forms of break. So those are different ways to employ hand-grips to up the volume of the breaking power of your heel-hooks. We're looking at the idea of breaking mechanics in the case of the straight Ashi-Urami family that we're looking at.

(34:18 - 34:34)
All of our best breaks occur out of the outside Ashi-Urami position. That's our preferred breaking position when we're dealing with very strong, talented opponents. It's okay just to go through the whole cycle with a Rumi Ashi-Urami or the less experienced opponents, that's fine.

(34:34 - 34:52)
We'll come back to that later. But when we're dealing with the world championship level, the toughest opponents, we typically go through a cycle of Ashi-Uramis until we arrive at the preferred breaking Ashi-Urami outside Ashi-Urami. Now, we've been talking a lot about grips.

(34:52 - 35:07)
We've been looking at a general theory of breaking. Now it's absolutely critical that we look at the function of our legs in breaking our opponent's legs. Remember, if you have to rely on your hands and arms to break people's legs, you're not going to have that much success.

(35:07 - 35:22)
Ultimately, the real horsepower of your breaking will come from your legs and your hips. It is critical you understand this. Now, you'll remember at the start of this DVD, we talked a little bit about the idea of levers and fulcrums.

(35:22 - 35:38)
This is where this kind of talk starts to become very, very useful. We saw that one of the important characteristics of a good breaking fulcrum in Jiu-Jitsu is the hardness and the placement of the fulcrum. The harder the fulcrum, the better.

(35:39 - 35:53)
If I try to break an object over a soft fulcrum, it's very, very difficult, often impossible. If I need to break a piece of wood, and I have a choice between putting it or breaking it over a deflated soccer ball versus a hard brick, of course, I'm going to choose the hard brick. It's a better breaking fulcrum.

(35:54 - 36:07)
So too with the human body. When I go to break someone's joints, I want to bring them over the bony, hard elements of my hip bones, not over the soft inner thighs. Heel hooking often employs this simple principle.

(36:09 - 36:35)
Let's look also at the critical notion of directionality of force. When I go to apply my Ashi-Gurami, the overwhelming majority of the breaking power of an outside Ashi-Gurami and a straight or outside heel hook comes from the inside leg. This is something that was not well understood for many, many years.

(36:36 - 36:50)
You have two legs in any given Ashi-Gurami. You have an inside leg between his legs, and an outside leg. I have to learn to cycle my use of inside and outside leg depending upon what I want to do.

(36:51 - 37:10)
When we looked at the initial system, a Rumi Ashi-Gurami, inside and outside leg are neutral. When I go to expose my opponent's heel, I have to use the outside leg as a dominant force. There's a cycle now of outside leg dominance that exposes my training partner's heel.

(37:11 - 37:29)
The critical mistake that most people made is that when they came to use their legs, they retained outside leg dominance when it was time to break people. And that was a terrible, terrible mistake. When my outside leg is dominant, it's pushing my opponent in the same direction as the escape.

(37:30 - 37:57)
And it means that when I go to apply the breaking force on the heel hook, my outside leg is alleviating pressure. As I try to turn my opponent's heel to the right, my outside leg is pushing in the same direction to my opponent's right, to my left. When I go to apply pressure, my dominant outside leg is actually assisting Kyle Masaru in the direction of his escape and making his job easier.

(37:58 - 38:14)
In addition, when I go to apply breaking force on the heel, my outside leg is actually pushing him in the same direction that I'm trying to apply breaking pressure. It's relieving pressure on my opponent's ankle. And that's why for so long, so many people attended heel hooks without results.

(38:15 - 38:29)
You have to learn to cycle inside leg and outside leg depending upon what you're trying to do. Yes, when I want to expose my opponent's heel, I need to be outside leg dominant. That's what exposes my training partner's heel.

(38:31 - 38:49)
When I go to follow my opponent's turn, yes, I should be outside leg dominant. But when I come over on the other side, it's all about the inside leg. The inside leg is now dominant and it pushes down on my training partner's knee in such a way that can affect the break.

(38:50 - 39:15)
Okay. Anytime I go to break my training partner's leg with a heel hook in the straight-out shigurami system, subsystem, my inside leg does the work of pushing in one direction against the work of my heel hook going in the opposite direction. This is a point so important that I'm going to spend some time on this right now.

(39:15 - 39:23)
Here's my inside leg. Here's my outside leg. We have the heel hook in place.

(39:23 - 39:40)
My inside leg must push to my right while my arms torque his ankle to my left. Only in this way can I exert a true breaking force on my opponent's legs. Watch my inside leg.

(39:41 - 39:51)
Okay. I take my inside leg and I dominate my opponent's knee with the inside leg. I push down on the knee.

(39:51 - 40:08)
If there's no push down on the knee and my legs are straight, then it's just my arms cranking to my left. There's some power, but nothing crazy. The moment my inside leg starts pushing down on the knee while I torque in the opposite direction with my arms, you're getting breaking force.

(40:09 - 40:24)
This is why it's easy for me to exert breaking force even with one hand. Okay. The inside knee pushes Kawamasaro's knee out to my right while my hand torques his ankle to my left.

(40:24 - 40:43)
In this way, you get a genuine rotating force on his lower leg from knee to ankle where his upper leg is going to the right and his lower leg is going to the left. In this way, we get true breaking pressure. So I have to learn in any heel hook cycle to cycle outside leg dominance with inside leg dominance.

(40:43 - 40:57)
So coming through from a basic ashi garami, look out now. I'm outside leg dominant. How do I know that? Because my knees are showing which direction we're pushing in.

(40:57 - 41:14)
My inside leg has come over and is pushing in this direction. As we go to applying force, everything switches with the outside ashi garami. Now my inside leg, you can clearly see my knees went from pointing to my left, they're now pointing to my right.

(41:15 - 41:34)
And as a result, pressure down with my left knee creates breaking pressure. So my left knee comes towards my right knee and the break is on. We've been looking at the straight ashi garami system.

(41:34 - 41:51)
We've seen that essentially it's composed of four distinct steps. Within that system, there are four distinct ashi garamis for each one of those steps. The idea is that we strongly distinguish between the mechanism of control, the ashi garami, and the mechanism of breaking the heel hook.

(41:51 - 42:07)
In this case, the outside heel hook. We've seen that our first step is always entry and connection. We use a ribby ashi garami as our method of entering our training partner's legs and forming a connection that puts my opponent's knee inside my knee line.

(42:07 - 42:28)
We've seen from here, my primary interest is in forming that connection and maintaining it against resistance. Once I see I've got an initial control of my opponent, it's time to go to the second phase of the system and that's to generate exposure of my opponent's heel. We've seen that a ribby ashi garami is not very appropriate for this task.

(42:28 - 42:49)
It's okay, you can do it against a beginner, but at highest levels, it's risky for me to start pitching down to a shoulder and exposing my own heel to my opponent as I'm digging for hits. So a better approach is to switch to another form of ashi garami. We chose inside ashi garami where I take my foot across the centre line and expose my opponent's heel.

(42:50 - 43:11)
From here, we get an initial grip, a false grip on our training partner like so. As a result, we now have the heel under our control and we're ready to go into the third stage of the system which is to negate my opponent's attempt at escape. I know what his three attempts are, they're always the same.

(43:11 - 43:35)
He can either break the structure of my ashi garami, he can rotate faster than I can rotate, or he can extract his knee from my knee line and play essentially a distance game. I can shut down all three of those by quickly locking a senkaku so that when he tries to peel my legs apart, one leg is reinforced by the other. I've got a closed system of wedges around both of his hips.

(43:35 - 43:58)
It's difficult for him to go after my heels because I've already got a grip on him while he has none on me. Okay, from here, I go to apply pressure on my training partner's heel that forces him into a roll. I know now he's going to go into the second phase of escape which is to try and use his foot on my buttocks and from here, push and get his knee free from my knee line.

(43:59 - 44:27)
But by pointing my knee down to the floor, even with no hands, the kakusai goes to run away from me so I never pull free. Once my knee goes down to the floor, this last thing is to try and back-step over the top but that leads right into the final phase, the outside ashi-gurami. We've seen from here a theory of breaking which involves legs, inside leg, dominating my opponent's knee.

(44:28 - 44:49)
Variations in hand grip, each one getting progressively tighter than the next. The idea of opposing forces, my ashi-gurami pointing in one direction and my heel hook going in the other. The idea of opposing forces on the foot itself, every heel hook is composed of a push on the toes and a pull on the heel.

(44:50 - 45:26)
We've seen that we put our opponent's toe line into the bicep, we put our wrist below the ankle and everything begins with a push on the toes and a pull on the heel. This leads to the concept of follow-through where even with no hands, I can generate tremendous pressure on my training partner by following through the breaking point. Once we work in this way, you see it's a fairly simple system and it can be done under stress against a wide variety of opponents in a very short period of time.

(45:27 - 45:49)
So for example, if I have someone in my butterfly guard, it's not a difficult thing for me in these circumstances to start lifting, elevating, popping over into an initial entry. From here, it's not a difficult thing. What I'll do with my training partner will come up and in a matter of seconds, we've got a very strong breaking position down the mat with an outside ashigurami.

(45:49 - 46:25)
It seems like a lot, four steps, four different ashiguramis, but really it all happens in a matter of seconds. A very useful way to teach, for me to teach you and for you to teach yourself the art of heel hooking in straight ashigurami scenarios is to look at the primary problem that you're trying to overcome. A big part of the way I teach you is to identify what's the main problem in any given scenario that you're trying to overcome in order to get to your goal.

(46:27 - 46:49)
The great problem associated with straight ashigurami attacks is always the problem of the shared spiral. An obvious question, what the hell is the problem of the shared spiral? Let's have a look at that right now. A heel hook of course is a twisting or spiralling form of lock.

(46:49 - 47:02)
There are some locks that are linear, so for example an amba, judogitami is a linear lock. Everything goes in a straight line. A heel hook like a kimura or a figure four toehold is a twisting lock, is a spiral of forces.

(47:04 - 47:25)
Whenever I go into a heel hook position, the force of my heel hook is followed by my opponent's escape route. They're exactly the same. So the problem is for most people there's a danger that as I go to spiral into the lock, he will spiral in the same direction to escape.

(47:25 - 47:38)
So we share the same spiral. And so you often get situations, you'll see this in competition all the time, where people just roll and roll and roll and they're both sharing the same spiral and nothing gets done. There's no result.

(47:38 - 47:55)
They just go out of bounds and get reset. I can never tolerate this. The problem with the shared spiral is how am I going to stop or curtail that spiralling process.

(47:55 - 48:17)
Yes, if my heel hook goes from right to left and my opponent's escape also goes from my right to my left, that's a potential problem. How do I overcome it? A large part of it has to do with the idea of cycling inside and outside leg dominance. We want to finish in an outside ashigurami to stop the cycle of the shared spiral.

(48:18 - 48:51)
If I retain the same ashigurami on my opponent as we go into work, my knees are pointing in the same direction as his knees. And as a result, we just keep on rolling together without result. But if I take my legs to an outside ashigurami position so that my knees are pointing across my training partner's knees or in extreme examples, even down towards the floor, as my opponent goes to continue the cycle of rolling, he'll break his own leg on my ashigurami.

(48:51 - 49:13)
I don't have to do a damn thing. So if my body is locked in this direction to my right hand side and Kawabusara goes to turn to my left, he'll break his own knee. There are some common errors made by many beginning students which will exasperate and make worse the problem of the shared spiral.

(49:13 - 49:39)
Let's look at the number one, which I always see when I go to teach heel hooking, which I want you to make sure you don't make this mistake. Probably the single most common mistake I see is people rolling over an elbow. When I go into a heel hook situation, I know I want to roll in this direction to ultimately end up in my outside heel hook, my outside ashigurami.

(49:39 - 49:59)
A very common mistake which you will see is people prop on an elbow. When they come up, they go to turn, they prop up on an elbow. This is a disaster because my own elbow will slow my rate of rotation so that when I go to follow Kawabusara's rotation, I go slower to him and he's gone.

(50:01 - 50:17)
Never when we go to roll do I prop myself on an elbow. I always lock my hands to my ribs and I roll smoothly over a shoulder. When I try to roll over an elbow, my elbow stops me and you feel very awkward.

(50:17 - 50:29)
You slow down, he keeps the same speed. If he's rotating faster than me, he will escape. I need to be able to rotate at the same speed he's rotating, which means rolling easily over my shoulders.

(50:29 - 50:39)
So let's look at an incorrect roll and a correct roll together. Here, I successfully employed the lock. Unfortunately, I've made a terrible mistake.

(50:39 - 50:58)
I've unlocked my hands and as Kawabusara goes to roll, I try to prop myself on my elbow, he rotated faster than me and easily escaped. Avoid this mistake. When we go to roll, always, once we lock up in this position, I roll over my shoulders.

(50:58 - 51:11)
Kawabusara, roll as fast as you can. So we're always in the right place. Now, we've created a situation where he goes to roll a second time he'll break his own leg.

(51:12 - 51:42)
Okay, because my Ashigurami is pointing against his roll as opposed to here pointing the same direction. Now, at the start of this DVD, we talked about the idea of reinforcing wedges. We saw that in the case of Ashigurami, I have to reinforce my wedges by locking my legs.

(51:42 - 52:09)
Either crossing my feet or locking triangles or Senkakus around my opponent's legs because body weight isn't a big part of the Ashigurami game. That was an oversimplification that I had to give to you to first get you around the idea of reinforcing wedges. But there is a way to bring in weight as a means of reinforcing wedges in Ashigurami and it pays big dividends when it's time to break someone's leg.

(52:09 - 52:41)
This is the idea of utilising the weight of my hips to reinforce the breaking power of my legs. From an outside Ashigurami situation, if my hips stay on the floor when I go to break my training partner's legs, it will put a cap or a ceiling on how far or how much pressure I can apply to my opponent's legs. When I go to break my opponent, I bring my knees down and the last component is for me to take my hips off the floor.

(52:42 - 53:06)
So I can get tremendous weight onto the top of my opponent's leg. If my hips are on the mat and I just pull up, it puts a limit on how much torque and how much follow-through I can get on my training partner's legs. When it's time to break, I lift my hips on top of my training partner's knees so that the bony hard fulcrum of my hips goes directly into the inside of his knee.

(53:06 - 53:18)
Now, I look down at the floor and I lift up in a straight line. So as my head rises, the yashi gurami pushes down. In this way, I've got weight down on the leg.

(53:18 - 53:29)
The only part of my body on the floor when I finish is my feet and my elbow. Everything else is off the mat. If my hips are on the floor, my weight gets transmitted to the floor.

(53:29 - 53:45)
When my hips come off the floor, my weight gets transmitted to my opponent's knee so that when I lift up on the heel, we get genuine breaking pressure. The same is true as my back is flat on the mat. As Kamasara goes to escape from the log, from here, I push down with my feet.

(53:46 - 53:57)
Because my hips are on the floor, it's putting a cap on how much power I can generate. But the moment my hips come off the mat, we get genuine breaking power. I cannot emphasise this enough.

(53:57 - 54:11)
When it's time to break, get your hips off the mat. Whether we're down on an elbow, like so, hips off the mat, breaking. Or whether we're flat on our back, hips off the mat, and breaking.

(54:12 - 54:18)
This will greatly amplify your breaking potential in any straight or outside Ashigurami situation.


## Volume 5

(0:03 - 0:21)
We've been looking at the Leglock system. We've seen that there's a general system composed of six essential skills arranged in a sequence. We've seen within that general system there's three distinct subsystems, each of which is significantly different from the other and has to be treated as a distinct entity.

(0:22 - 0:44)
We looked at the straight Ashigurami system and saw that it was composed mainly around the idea of multiple Ashiguramis for multiple tasks. There were four distinct tasks of, if you're looking for that position, four distinct Ashiguramis required to fulfil those tasks and a central problem to overcome. The central problem is the idea of shared spirals.

(0:44 - 1:22)
Now we're going to switch to the second and one of the more well-known parts of the Leglock system, which is the subsystem known as Cross Ashigurami. Cross Ashigurami represents an important element of the Leglock system overall and for the development of the Leglock system. You all know that Brazilian Jiu-Jitsu is based around the notion of hierarchy, that there are certain movements, pins in the sport, which are rated more highly than others.

(1:22 - 1:43)
And this is reflected in the point system. So, for example, knee on belly gets a certain number of points, mounted position gets more, rear mounted position gets more. Certain positions are valued more highly because they offer, if we were in a real fight situation, a greater ability to control my opponent in ways where I could strike him on the ground.

(1:45 - 2:06)
The Leglock system also is hierarchical in exactly the same fashion, only now we're not concerned with punching people on the ground, we're concerned with a different set of criteria. When I go to judge the worth of a Lock and an Ashigurami, there's two main things that I concern myself with. The first is safety.

(2:07 - 2:26)
Whenever I go into any form of Ashigurami, regardless of type, I can't do it without exposing my own feet to my opponent. So, if I get lackadaisical with my foot position, I may well be finished myself here with an inverted heel hook. If I get lackadaisical with my foot position here, I can be finished in toe holds.

(2:28 - 2:58)
Anytime I go into an Ashigurami, my own feet are exposed to my opponent, just as his feet are exposed to mine. The question is always, what degree of exposure is there? Certain kinds of Ashigurami result in greater degrees of foot exposure. So, for example, a 50-50 type situation can often result in undesirable exposure of my own feet to my opponent's feet, depending upon my skill level and his skill level.

(2:59 - 3:37)
As a general rule, I rate an Ashigurami higher or lower, depending upon two things. How much are my feet exposed to danger from my opponent's counters? And what is the breaking potential of the locks employed from that Ashigurami? In the case of cross Ashigurami, it can be very risky for me to put my feet into situations where we both have more or less the same lock upon each other. I can definitely break my opponent from this position, but he can also definitely break me if his skill level is good.

(3:38 - 4:11)
In general, I like to work from situations where there is minimal exposure of my feet to him, while there is maximal exposure of his feet to me. As a result, of all the various Ashiguramis that I work with, the one which is the highest, the apex of the hierarchy, is a cross Ashigurami, where my feet are positioned on the inside of my opponent's legs. This minimises the exposure of my feet to my opponent, and it maximises my ability to go in and break my opponent.

(4:14 - 4:33)
It's no secret that probably the most devastating breaks in the sport of Judo-su come from inside heel hooks. In top-level competition, they have been probably the number one method in the entire leg-locking game for getting submissions in high-level competition. There is a reason for this.

(4:34 - 4:58)
Mechanically, when I go in to attack my opponent's legs in situations from a cross Ashigurami, the target of my attack is the inside of my opponent's knee, the medial ligaments. In addition, the distance through which I have to move in order to break my opponent's leg is extremely small. So, the target of the attack is a significant one.

(4:58 - 5:13)
If I tear my opponent's MCL, it's hard, difficult for my opponent to keep on fighting. And the distance that's required in order for me to enact the break is extremely small. And there's a third consideration.

(5:14 - 5:29)
In a cross Ashigurami, both of my opponent's feet and legs are in front of me. Contrast this with a straight Ashigurami. Now, only one of my opponent's legs is in front of me.

(5:29 - 5:48)
The other is behind. I can't access both of my opponent's legs at the same time. In addition, when I go to apply breaking pressure on my opponent, the force of my break is mostly transmitted into the LCL, the outside of my opponent's knee, and to a lesser degree, the ankle.

(5:49 - 6:06)
These are strong and powerful breaks, but they are not the same as taking the MCL as you would with an inside heel hook. Your opponent can go on to fight in many cases, even with a severe tear of the LCL. But it's extremely difficult for him to do so with a complete tear of the MCL.

(6:08 - 6:37)
In addition, the range of motion through which I have to move is typically greater in the case of straight or outside heel hooks than it is in the case of cross Ashigurami inside heel hooks. So, for reasons like this, my preferred position for breaking is always a cross Ashigurami inside heel hook with my feet to the inside. We're going to be seeing soon that there are many varieties of foot position on the inside.

(6:38 - 6:47)
I can head my feet foot over foot, like so. I can lock a triangle with my legs. The so-called inside Senkaku.

(6:48 - 7:22)
I can also take my foot from one kind of Senkaku, my foot in the middle of my opponent, to my foot around on the other side, so that I have wedges on both sides of my opponent's legs. This is a very, very strong form of block. A method which my student Eddie Cummings specialised in was the use of the shoelaces as a strong wedge to push him against his opponent's hip.

(7:23 - 7:41)
That was something that he certainly used with a lot of effect in competition. I won't teach that, since that was Eddie Cummings' innovation, not mine. But I will teach the various other forms of inside Senkaku, which my students have used with tremendous success in competition.

(7:42 - 8:04)
Let's start off with a very important distinction, which becomes crucial in your understanding of the cross-Ashigurami position. This is the distinction between a primary and a secondary leg. In any situation where I have an Ashigurami, regardless of cross or straight, there's always a primary leg.

(8:04 - 8:17)
That's the leg held by the Ashigurami. So if I have my Ashigurami around Bernardo's left leg, the left leg is the primary leg. The other leg is the secondary leg.

(8:19 - 8:37)
Almost all of my opponent's escapes to any given Ashigurami originate with the secondary leg. So, for example, Bernardo could take his foot and pommel it inside my shin. Pommel through and around and threaten me.

(8:38 - 9:01)
The escape started with this leg, the secondary leg. The beauty of the cross-Ashigurami position is that it puts both the primary and the secondary leg directly in front of me, where I can access both at the same time. This is not true in the case of the straight Ashiguramis that we looked at previously.

(9:01 - 9:17)
Here, the primary leg is in front of me, but the secondary leg is behind me. As a result, my opponent can go into a lot of strong defensive movements based on the movements of this leg. That all starts to change when we go cross-Ashi.

(9:18 - 9:44)
From a cross-Ashi system, both the primary leg, the one held by my Ashigurami, and the secondary leg are right there in front of me. I can access both of them very, very easily. This leads us into a very special form of Ashigurami that my students often make use of, the ankle-lace Ashigurami, where they seize the secondary leg and hold it with an Achilles lock, just like so.

(9:45 - 10:02)
From a position like this, my opponent's legs are laced together, in the same way that you would manacle a prisoner by handcuffing his ankles together. When my opponent goes to move, it's so difficult for him to generate any kind of effective movement. He's more or less held in place.

(10:02 - 10:24)
This is essentially a form of pin without body weight. Whenever we go into this position, we can hold people for long periods of time, and then control the timing of the break. So when we're in the cross-Ashigurami position, one of our favourite methods of working is to get a hold of our opponent's secondary leg and lace the ankles.

(10:25 - 10:54)
This laced leg Ashigurami is a big, big part of the cross-Ashigurami system, and we'll be spending a lot of time in that area. In fact, it'll be the first part of our focus. We've seen that the leg-lock system is really an amalgamation of three, very separate, and yet linked, subsystems.

(10:55 - 11:17)
The straight Ashigurami system, the cross-Ashigurami system, and the reverse Ashigurami system. Let's look now at the cross-Ashigurami system, and start to understand some of the similarities and differences between it and other systems that we work with. A classy dog is sitting in front of me, and his leg goes across my centre line.

(11:17 - 11:27)
His right leg across my right hip. This will be, obviously, a cross-Ashigurami situation. As a general rule, we like to work with our feet inside our training partner's legs.

(11:27 - 11:56)
It is possible in situations like this to work with your feet on the outside, but now there's increasing vulnerability on my part from my opponent's attacks, and a general lessening in control, and an ability to switch off into other options that we want to avoid. So, as a general rule, we much prefer to have our feet in the inside position, just like so. Now, you guys are familiar with some of the concepts we work with with regards to the straight Ashigurami system.

(11:56 - 12:08)
Let's look at some obvious similarities between straight Ashigurami and cross-Ashigurami. One thing that doesn't change is the notion of knee and knee line. Regardless of what kind of heel hook I'm going to attempt, one thing always remains unchanged.

(12:09 - 12:25)
I have to keep my opponent's knee inside my knee line. We're defining the knee line as a straight line that joins my left and right knee, and Placido's knee must remain within that line. So, a very important thing in situations like this is to be able to maintain that knee line relationship.

(12:26 - 12:51)
One of our favourite ways to work in this situation is to control the secondary leg, like so. If I see Placido begin to move out of this position, I will take my arm and I will put an extra wedge underneath my training partner's primary leg and lock in, just like so. When he tries to shuffle out now, that extra wedge means that at any given moment, I can always bring his knee back inside my knee line and lock up more securely.

(12:51 - 13:13)
So, if you're in this situation, the cross Ashigurami, and you feel your opponent is doing a good job of slipping his knee, and you feel distances increasing between you, this is the time to take your arm, go elbow deep around your training partner's knee to put that extra wedge in. So, when he tries to go further out, it's very, very difficult. And now I can use that extra wedge to draw him back inside my knee line and go back into work.

(13:14 - 13:35)
So, this obviously is a similarity with the previous Ashiguramis that we looked at in a straight Ashigurami system. Nonetheless, there are also some very important differences. One of the more noticeable differences, when we get into a cross Ashigurami situation, is that for the first time, we now have two legs in front of us.

(13:35 - 14:17)
In a straight Ashigurami situation, we always have, regardless of the Ashigurami, one leg in front of us, and one leg behind us. All that changes in a cross Ashigurami situation, where now, for the first time, we have two legs in front. There's going to be many advantages to that.

This is one reason why we strongly favour this position. We also find the nature of the heel hook itself, the inside heel hook, is substantially different. In an inside heel hook, the primary stress on my training partner's body when I go into a heel hook is going to be on the MCL, as opposed to a straight Ashigurami system, where the primary stress is on the LCL, on the outside of the leg.

(14:17 - 14:33)
So we tend to get slightly more catastrophic breaks on our opponent's leg. So in heated situations, where you need to maximise breaking power, we generally have a preference for inside heel hooks out of a cross Ashigurami. So you see there's some similarities, some differences, when we go to this position.

(14:38 - 14:54)
We're looking at the cross Ashigurami system. Let's add in some preliminary thoughts that will be very, very important as we go through this video. We said earlier that when we work in a cross Ashigurami situation, we've got two legs in front of us.

(14:54 - 15:05)
Let's make a very, very important distinction between a primary and a secondary leg. Let's define the primary leg as the leg which is actually held by my Ashigurami. That's the one that's caught between my two legs.

(15:06 - 15:27)
We call it the primary leg because ultimately what we want to do is we want to get to the strongest heel hook in the entire sport of Jiu-Jitsu, which is the inside heel hook. Ultimately, we're looking to get to this grip. However, we have to understand that around 90% of my opponent's escape potential comes from his secondary leg.

(15:27 - 15:45)
That's what's going to enable him to pull his foot inside my feet, and then from here go into attack knee with counters. That's what's going to enable him to start turning and spinning in this direction to get away from my Ashigurami. Most of his escape potential comes from the secondary leg, whereas most of my submission potential comes on the primary leg.

(15:46 - 15:59)
When we get into this position, the primary leg is the one we're going to be attacking. The secondary leg is the one we typically use to bind up to get control over a difficult, unmanageable opponent. Let's always keep that in mind as we work.

(15:59 - 16:26)
When we talk about a primary leg, that's the one that's held inside our Ashigurami, and the secondary leg is the other leg, which usually generates most of our opponent's escape potential. We're looking at the cross Ashigurami subsystem within the general leg lock system. I'm going to talk now about a very, very important distinction in how we go to operate in this position.

(16:27 - 16:42)
There's a basic split in two methods that I can work out of any cross Ashigurami situation. I would like it very much if we're being proficient at both. I do have a preference, but that doesn't mean that you should only specialise in one or the other.

(16:44 - 16:54)
There are absolutely going to be times where you have to work with the other option, even if you have a preferred option. It very much pays to be proficient in both. Let's have a look at the distinction.

(16:55 - 17:14)
Whenever I work in a cross Ashigurami system, we've seen there's two legs in front of us. How I go to operate in this situation will come down to two fundamental choices. Will I work attacking both of my opponent's legs, or will I work with one leg? Single leg versus double leg options, essentially.

(17:15 - 17:24)
My preference is always to control both legs. I like the control that comes with this. I like the fact that I can slow the match down and limit my training partner's movements.

(17:25 - 17:37)
But I would be a liar if I said to you, you're always going to be able to get two legs. You're going to have many opponents who do a good job of splitting their legs and starting moving away. Now I have to work with one leg, and I should still be able to be successful under those circumstances.

(17:39 - 17:57)
We're going to coach this position as though there were a split between those two options. Because how I go to operate is entirely dependent on which of those two options is open to me. I'm going to coach first my preference, which is to work with the principle of double trouble and lock up both of my opponent's legs.

(17:58 - 18:18)
Then we're going to come back later to the idea of working with a single leg and still be able to get to a successful submission without the use of the second leg. But our first line of direction in investigating the cross-archer throwing position is going to be to work with two legs, the double leg scenario. Working with double trouble, where we bind up our training partner's legs like so.

(18:19 - 18:40)
Then later on, we'll come back to the single leg situation with the other leg. The secondary leg is too far away from me, and now I have to go to work on one leg to get the submission. You guys know that a central tenet of my teaching philosophy in Jiu-Jitsu is what we call central problem theory.

(18:40 - 19:09)
That for any given scenario or position in the sport of Jiu-Jitsu, regardless of what it is, there's always at least one central problem that motivates both me as the person performing the action and my opponent as the person resisting the action. If you show me any given position or scenario in the sport of Jiu-Jitsu, I can always identify at least one central problem that will motivate the actions of the two athletes. Let's look at how this occurs in the case of cross-archery running.

(19:10 - 19:38)
Whenever we're here, the fundamental problem that we face in this situation is what I refer to as the turnout problem. Let's see how the turnout problem materialises and how it drives the action in a given direction once the athletes get into this position. If my opponent were naive and he turned into me, that would be a terrible mistake on his part, because essentially what he's done is he's exposed his own heel to my inside heel hook.

(19:40 - 20:04)
Here, Placido starts in a neutral position, but by turning into me, he's clearly exposed the heel without me having to do any work. Even if he high-legged over and stepped over, it would not help, because his high leg will never beat the power of a closed wedge inside Senkaku. I can return him to the mat, into the heel hook, or if he's so physically strong that he maintains his position, I'll just break him right here.

(20:05 - 20:30)
Even his entire moving body weight won't break the strength of my Senkaku around his leg, so that would be a terrible mistake on his part. Another common mistake is people will move backwards in straight lines. You know what they're trying to do.

It's not a bad idea on their part. They're trying to take their knee outside of my knee line, but I have a closed wedge around their legs. When he moves back, it's so easy for me just to stay stuck to him.

(20:30 - 20:58)
As he moves back, he's doing nothing to hide his heel, and as a result, we can easily go in and get the break. So movements straight backwards are ineffective. Movements into me are worse than ineffective.

They're actually positively hazardous. So what's the right thing for him to do? It's always to begin an action of turning out in this direction. Even if I had a heel hook coming back, Placido, he would fight my hands, split my hands, slip his heel, and then begin to turn out.

(20:59 - 21:05)
Once Placido engages in a turn out, interesting things start to happen. Let's look at the first. Come back.

(21:05 - 21:23)
From here, as long as my head is inside my training partner's legs, I can access the heel. The moment my head goes outside his legs, it's very, very difficult for me now to start accessing the heel. By turning in this direction, that's exactly what Placido is doing.

(21:23 - 21:43)
He's putting my head outside of his legs, and as a result, it's getting harder and harder for me to access his heel. The only way I can try is to try and come down to an elbow, and that unweights my leg. It makes me very vulnerable to a simple thumb post, and now Placido can easily slip his legs out, and not only escape, he can actually start countering me in this position.

(21:44 - 21:51)
Now I can end up in my own cross-archery ground. I don't have to fight my way out of that. So this is always the problem associated with the turn out escape.

(21:53 - 22:15)
Whenever someone goes to turn away from me, it does a good job of first hiding the heel, and then unweighting one of my major archery ground legs, the outside leg, and from here, it's an easy knee slip, I've no longer controlled the knee line, and he's gone. The heel is lost. So the smart thing for him to do, whether I have the heel or not, is always to turn out in this direction.

(22:15 - 22:40)
That simple insight will motivate both his actions and my actions in this position. Everything we do in the cross-archery ground position, and everything you will do as a student, will be motivated and given direction by this simple insight. The whole cross-archery ground system which I teach is essentially a response to this one central problem.

(22:41 - 23:03)
We will soon see that the central problem, the turn out problem, creates spin-off problems, which we will have to confront as we go further into the system. But whether I choose to work with a single leg, or whether I choose to work with a double leg, is essentially a response to this problem. How I go to act will be an attempt to overcome this turn out problem.

(23:08 - 23:21)
We're looking at the cross-archery ground system. Let's get further into it now. In a situation where I'm locked up with Placido and a cross-archery ground, we've seen there's a central problem that we're always trying to overcome, the turn out problem.

(23:22 - 23:36)
My personal favourite response to the turn out problem always comes back to the principle of double trouble. I know in order to turn out his secondary leg, the knee must go away from me and point away. It has to happen.

(23:37 - 23:52)
So my number one thing that I favour above all others is to go into a different form of Ashigurami and seize my opponent's secondary leg. You will often see my students make use of this principle. All of them do it extremely well, and all of them have had tremendous success with it.

(23:53 - 24:27)
The first issue that I face here is not the issue of breaking my opponent. It's of controlling his movement. His movement is designed to get him away, turning out in this direction, and I've got to do something to address that.

I have to address this. I can't go fishing for a heel hook when the question of movement and control hasn't been resolved yet. This is not the time for heel hooking.

This is the time for stopping movement. As soon as I get into a position like this and I see any kind of movement away, my number one thing is to straighten my leg and get a two-on-one grip and snatch my opponent, one above the knee and one below the knee. Now let's be very careful how we grip here.

(24:28 - 24:38)
Do not go in superficially with your fingers. I need to go wrist-deep around my opponent's leg. That means both of my wrists have to bend at least 90 degrees.

(24:39 - 24:48)
I'm looking not to grip the leg. I'm looking to grip the knee tendon underneath here. I want to take four fingers and lock in here.

(24:49 - 24:59)
Four fingers here. Once again, as he goes to turn away, I reach and grip. Once I get my grip, I draw everything back and lock it up with an Achilles hold here.

(24:59 - 25:04)
I'm not trying to put any heat on his leg at this point. I'm not trying to hurt him with this look. I just hold him in place.

(25:05 - 25:12)
When he goes to turn in this direction, only the upper body can turn. I don't care about the upper body in this situation. I care about the lower body.

(25:12 - 25:19)
His upper body can turn here, absolutely. But the part that counts isn't turning anywhere. He won't be able to complete a turnout.

(25:20 - 25:34)
I can hold someone here for long periods of time. We've seen even if you lose the knee line in this situation, you can recover it here very, very easily by putting an extra wedge in and drawing him back. If I want my opponent to move in any given direction, I can do it.

(25:34 - 25:44)
If I want him to fall this way, it's an easy thing to make him do. If I want my opponent to roll over in this direction, it's an easy thing for me to accomplish. I control someone here at this point.

(25:46 - 26:02)
This is absolutely one of the most controlling positions that I know of in the sport of Jiu-Jitsu. I have the same amount of control over someone here as I would in the mounted position or the rear mounted position. I completely control the direction and capacity for movement that my opponent has.

(26:03 - 26:14)
You can see what's behind this. Essentially what you've done is you've shackled your training partner's two ankles together. It's like if you want to control an unruly prisoner, what's a great thing to do is to handcuff his two ankles together.

(26:15 - 26:19)
He's not going to run away at all. He's completely controlled. That's essentially what I've done here.

(26:19 - 26:43)
Whatever athleticism my opponent has is destroyed by the fact that his two ankles are shackled together by this grip. Essentially my response, my favourite response I should say, to the turn out problem is to make a switch to a new form of Ashigurami. I had a basic bare bones cross Ashigurami and I went across and I took a lace leg or ankle lace Ashigurami.

(26:44 - 27:02)
Where I took a hold of my training partner's leg and laced his two ankles together. The moment I get to this position, I know I control my opponent's movement in a way which no other Ashigurami can do. We've seen that there's a fundamental problem here, a central problem, the turn out problem.

(27:02 - 27:13)
We've seen my favourite response to that is to switch to a new form of Ashigurami, the ankle lace Ashigurami. Which more or less prevents a turn out. Now we know something here.

(27:14 - 27:32)
Ultimately we want to push ourselves to the top of the breaking hierarchy. And we know the top of the hierarchy for breaking and leg locking is this, the inside heel hook. As good as the ankle lace Ashigurami is for controlling people, there's an obvious problem here.

(27:32 - 27:44)
If I hold on to the secondary leg, how am I going to heel hook the primary leg? At some point I've got to release this leg. And that's when things start getting interesting. Now we get a new derivative problem.

(27:45 - 28:00)
The central problem we faced was always the turn out problem. We solved that by switching to a new form of Ashigurami, the lace leg or ankle lace Ashigurami. At some point if I want to heel hook this man, I have to release the secondary leg.

(28:01 - 28:11)
The moment I go to release the secondary leg, the whole turn out problem emerges anew. And now there's a race to get to the heel. I often refer to this as the release problem.

(28:12 - 28:19)
When I go to release my opponent's leg, the turn out problem re-emerges. So here I've stopped the turn out. Now I want to get my heel hook.

(28:19 - 28:31)
I release and it's gone again. Learning to overcome the release problem is one of the keys to success to this position. And it's something you will have to become highly proficient at.

(28:31 - 28:50)

(28:50 - 29:07)
We've seen there's a central problem, a turn out problem. But this creates a new problem, a follow up problem as it were.

(29:08 - 29:21)
The release problem. When I go to perform a heel hook I have to release my opponent's secondary leg and then he can turn out again. So we're going to look now at managing the release of our opponent's secondary leg so we can get through to some successful breaks.

(29:21 - 29:32)

(29:55 - 30:09)

(30:53 - 31:05)

Remember, ultimately, this whole battle is a battle over heel exposure. I want to expose his heel, and obviously he wants the exact opposite.

(45:22 - 45:38)
The whole reason why he turns out is to hide his heel from me. What I want is this. An exposed heel with my head inside his legs, so I can go into the strongest lock in the entire leg lock system.

(45:38 - 45:54)
That's what I want, that's what he wants. Now, for me to have a control over the release of the secondary leg essentially means this. I have to have one arm at the wrist around his heel.

(45:54 - 46:15)
If I have that, absolutely, I can release my opponent's secondary leg and be very confident of a successful break. So let's start looking now at how we can do this. From a situation where I have a hold of my opponent's secondary leg, I'm going to place what we call a splitter hand in between my training partner's two legs.

(46:16 - 46:32)
I'm going to lift the secondary leg, and I'm going to place a forearm between my training partner's two legs. It functions as a splitter, splitting his two legs apart, as opposed to here where the legs are in contact with each other. So I lift and place the splitter hand in.

(46:32 - 46:45)
The moment I get the splitter hand in, I'm going to take my free arm and I'm going to go around the corner, and I'm going to lock up right here. This creates an interesting situation. This creates an interesting situation.

(46:48 - 47:06)
I have my opponent's heel. If my opponent were naive and he thought, Hey, there's nothing holding my secondary leg in place, I'll take it out and go to turn out, he'll break himself because I already have the heel in place. Let's have a look at that again.

(47:07 - 47:20)
I'm in position, I lift and I insert the splitter hand. I go through and lock up. My opponent's been telling himself this whole time, If I turn out, I'm going to be safe.

(47:21 - 47:31)
That's no longer true, because my hands are locked in the heel hook. If my opponent extracts his foot now and goes to turn out, I don't even have to do any work. His turning motion will break his leg.

(47:31 - 47:42)
And when I add my force to it, I'll double that. This is where things get interesting. We now run into the third big problem associated with cross-axial ground.

(47:42 - 48:01)
Once I get my opponent's heel locked in place like this, a new problem emerges. I often call this the interference problem or the two-foot problem. In order to break someone's leg in an inside heel hook, I must have one foot inside my armpit to get a satisfactory break.

(48:02 - 48:13)
If there are two feet inside my armpit, one foot forms a kind of interference upon the other and you never get truly satisfactory breaks. Yes, you can hurt people in this position. Yes, you can cause pain.

(48:14 - 48:25)
But at world championship level, people aren't going to tap to pain or discomfort. They're going to tap only because there's the threat of a severe break. And it's very, very hard for me to severely break someone's leg when there's two feet in my armpit.

(48:26 - 48:42)
One foot characteristically interferes with the breaking of the other. So, if I have a knowledgeable, smart opponent, someone who knows the legal game pretty well, and I put a split of hand in, and I come in here and lock, he's not going to turn out. He's going to realise that's suicide at this point.

(48:43 - 48:54)
Instead, he's going to push his foot into my armpit to amplify the interference. Okay? How do we deal with this? I'll show you right now. I'm going to pitch over onto my side.

(48:55 - 49:05)
As Placido thrusts the foot in, watch my left knee. I'm going to place my left knee just inside his knee. So, I repositioned it over his hip to the knee.

(49:06 - 49:24)
Once my knee goes inside, I can use that as a pushing force. I pitch over onto my side, and I push with my left knee into his left knee. And as a result, the secondary leg pops out like a champagne cork, but leaves the primary leg in place.

(49:25 - 49:39)
And as a result, I can fall back now and go into a very, very strong break. Okay? Essentially, what I'm doing is I'm using my legs to remove his secondary leg when I already have a heel hook in place. Okay? Don't make this common mistake.

(49:41 - 49:47)

(49:47 - 49:56)

(49:57 - 50:13)
Okay? I have to use my legs to move him. I must pitch over to the side, and then position my knee, and now I hold the heel hook tight, and my left knee pushes his secondary leg out. When he goes to turn out now, no.

(50:13 - 50:24)

(1:01:45 - 1:02:02)

(1:02:03 - 1:02:20)
As he goes to pull away from me from here, both ankles are well wedged in place. I don't care whether you just go down with the arm or physically lock an Achilles lock. (1:02:20 - 1:02:31)
I take my thumb, and I go back, and I touch my own back. Here's a common problem. Sometimes I coach athletes who say they have a difficult time securing the heel in this position.

(1:02:32 - 1:02:51)
It's usually because their head and body are leaning too far back, and as a result, they cannot get to the heel. Whenever you see this problem, simply sit up and forward by employing a forward shift so that my head goes to the same height as your training partner's head. As a result, I can always reach back and secure my training partner's heel.

(1:02:52 - 1:03:17)
If I have to, I will use the strength of both legs to draw him back so his knee line goes back, and I have the heel in place. Then from here, we go over the top, release, and finish. (1:03:18 - 1:03:44)
You're in the perfect position to renew your attacks. Simply by throwing your legs over the top, you can go right back in on the other side. Anytime I take my opponent's arms through in this situation and bring the leg across my body, anytime I lose my opponent's primary leg, his secondary leg will simply become your new primary leg, and you can make effortless switches from one leg to the other.
