(1:04:28 - 1:04:39)
So far, everything we've looked at has involved the use of both of our opponent's legs. We're appealing to the principle of double trouble. This is my personal favourite way of operating anytime we're in cross-ashigurami.

(1:04:39 - 1:05:02)
It's incredibly controlling, and essentially converts your ashigurami to a method of pinning your opponent's movement. As I said earlier, you'll see all of my students using this repeatedly in top international competition, and they're able to hold some of the biggest, strongest, and most talented opponents for long periods of time and work their way methodically for the break. It's an incredibly effective way of controlling people's movement out of ashigurami.

(1:05:03 - 1:05:26)
However, there are always going to be times where you cannot access the secondary leg, and you're going to have to go directly on the attack with one leg only under your control. This substantially changes the way we operate in this position. Because it means now, I don't really have a method of stopping my opponent from turning out.

(1:05:27 - 1:05:40)
So instead of stopping the turning out, I have to learn to control the turnout. Essentially, when I say control the turnout, it means controlling the speed or the pace of the turnout. Let's have a look at what I mean by this.

(1:05:42 - 1:05:53)
We've seen that the central problem in any cross ashigurami is the turnout problem. I would love to have heel exposure and get an inside heel hook on my opponent. He knows that, so of course he starts to turn.

(1:05:54 - 1:06:08)
When he starts to turn, he positions my head in the one place that will make me ineffective in this position. My head is now outside his legs. To be effective in this position, I need my head inside my opponent's legs.

(1:06:09 - 1:06:23)
Only then can I easily access the heel. The happiest times are when your opponent turns into you, because then he actually physically puts your head inside the legs and gives you the heel. But smart opponents know that, and so they're always turning away from you.

(1:06:24 - 1:06:52)
If I had the secondary leg, then we could use all the methods that we saw previously to go in and successfully complete the attack. But if I don't have the leg, what do I do now? Well, essentially what I have to do is I have to get my head back inside the legs, and I have to slow down the pace of my opponent's turn so that ultimately my turn is faster than his. I have to play catch-up so that I can get to the heel.

(1:06:53 - 1:07:07)
The problem is, most of the time, he goes first. He's the escaping guy, he bolts off, and you have to play catch-up. A very, very important concept whenever we work in this position is very simple to state.

(1:07:07 - 1:07:17)
Essentially what's going on here is a race. It's a race for my head position inside his legs. If I can win that race, I can access the heel.

(1:07:19 - 1:07:29)
Let's understand something. There's two ways to win a race of any kind. I can make myself faster, or I can make the other guy slower.

(1:07:30 - 1:07:40)
Usain Bolt is the fastest man in the world. I'm a 51-year-old cripple with a hip replacement. If we get into a 100-metre race, of course I'm going to lose very, very badly.

(1:07:41 - 1:07:56)
But if you let me shackle Usain Bolt's legs together and tie his hands behind his back, even I can win in a race of that nature. It's not because I got faster, it's because I made the other guy slower. Most of Jiu-Jitsu is built along those lines.

(1:07:57 - 1:08:22)
It's about making your opponent slower rather than yourself faster. How does this work in this case? How do I make Placido slower so that I can control the pace of the roll? Let's look at that right now. Whenever I'm in this position and I see my opponent's getting away, the first thing I like to do is put on a handbrake, where I put my left hand around my opponent's knee and I physically hold the knee tendons.

(1:08:22 - 1:08:50)
Don't just put your hand here superficially. That's not going to slow anybody down. I put my fingers all the way through and I bend my wrist 90 degrees so that it functions as a handbrake.

I close my elbow, so when Placido goes to turn, it won't stop him, but it will slow him down. The next thing I do is I take my elbow and I put it here, in the inside position. So I have a handbrake and an elbowbrake.

(1:08:51 - 1:09:00)
Now, the next part is, in my opinion, the most important. This is a head post. As we move over, I'm going to put my head on the floor.

(1:09:02 - 1:09:17)
This creates a barrier for my opponent to roll over and slows everything down dramatically. The fourth ingredient is my left foot. It hooks here, inside my training partner's left knee.

(1:09:19 - 1:10:48)
When he tries to pull his body around to continue his turn, the combination of my head, my leg, my hand, and my elbow dramatically slows him down, so that when I start to turn, I get to the heel every time, because my turn was faster than his. Not because I'm fast, but because I slowed him down. Let's have a look at that again.

Essentially here, we have a race. The race is for him to move in this direction and put my head outside of his legs, knowing that if he succeeds in doing so, I'll never access his heel. What I need to do is slow things down.

I take my hand and I go in here and lock. I put my elbow in place. Then, as we go into the turn, I put my head on the floor and my second foot locks in here at the knee.

When he goes to complete the turn, it slows him down dramatically. It means that I can get my head inside and access the heel, so that as we emerge on the outside of the roll, I have a heel in place and a potential break. Essentially what we're doing here is we're identifying the problem in the single leg situation as a shared spiral, just as it was with the outside heel.

(1:10:49 - 1:10:59)
We share the same spiral. We're spiralling in the same direction, and I have to control the speed of the spiral. In other words, my spiral has to go faster than his.

(1:11:00 - 1:11:09)
I don't rely on my speed to get it done. I rely on my ability to slow him down. Let's have a look one more time at this important sequence.

(1:11:12 - 1:11:21)
Once we get into a situation like this, and I don't have my opponent's secondary leg, the first thing I'll do is that. I'll put in a hand break. The second thing I'll do is this.

(1:11:22 - 1:11:35)
Even as he begins to turn, it's not as fast as he wanted it to be. Then I put my head down the floor, and my leg goes across. So that even as we emerge out the other side, the heel hook is on.

(1:11:36 - 1:12:18)
Now from here, my hands connect, and we get to a successful break. This is my favourite way to deal with the cross-ashi gurame in situations where we have only one leg under our control. Essentially, I put a series of basic, simple, mechanical breaks on my opponent.

My hand, my elbow position, my head post on the floor, and my interfering foot in his leg. This slows my opponent down sufficiently that I can win the race for head position. Get my head inside my opponent's legs, and therefore access the heel.

So that as we shear the spiral and roll through, I come out at the end of the spiral, ahead of my opponent, with a fully locked in inside heel hook.


## Volume 6

(0:02 - 11:58)
We'll be looking at the leg lock system we've seen as a general leg lock system and within that general system there are three subsystems, cross hashigurami, the straight hashigurami and reverse hashigurami. One of the most important points that we're trying to make as we work through this instructional series is the idea that for any given scenario, whether it be cross, straight or reverse hashigurami, there's always going to be one central problem in that scenario which guides and informs and directs the action between the two athletes. So we have Karl and Bernardo in a cross hashigurami situation.

It's well known what the two athletes want to do in this situation. Bernardo wants to expose his opponent's heel and control the two legs right around his opponent's leg. He can have his legs in a variety of configurations.

Karl Osaro's intention is always to prevent heel exposure and he does that by turning away. This creates the so-called turnout problem. From Bernardo's perspective the whole question of how he's going to successfully deal with this situation comes down to will he be able to overcome the turnout problem.

One way he can do this as we saw earlier is to control the secondary leg that prevents his opponent from turning out. But when he goes to release the secondary leg, now the same problem occurs. This is the so-called release problem that we spent some time investigating.

In a situation where we have a straight hashigurami, Karl will do a straight hashigurami, release, release. Karl does a straight hashigurami on Bernardo. Here it was a different problem.

When Karl Osaro goes to apply a heel hook, all the pressure is going in this direction and now we run into the problem of a shared spiral. So both athletes start spiralling in the same direction. If they just continue to spiral, nothing really results.

It's not a successful break. So there has to be some kind of dealing with this central problem, the problem of shared spirals. We're going to start looking now at reverse hashigurami, the third of the three subsystems in the general system.

We're going to see that there's a new problem that starts emerging in this interesting and different position. And this problem will motivate most of the ways we go to act and behave in this position. First and secondly, what the reverse hashigurami is.

Almost all the various hashiguramis involve two athletes facing each other. Reverse hashigurami is the only one where that's not true. I face the same direction as my opponent is facing.

In a situation where my opponent is down, we sit on top of our training partner and we have both legs in front of us. There's good news and bad news here. The good news is you have easy access to both of your opponent's legs.

That's a good thing. That's working with the principle of double travel, that both of my opponent's legs can be controlled at the same time. The bad news is my opponent's legs can easily be locked in a triangle or crossed feet or any other configuration he wants so that one leg can defend the other.

The fundamental problem of this position is always the problem of crossed legs. I want a leg lock of some kind on the leg that's between my legs. I have an hashigurami, two legs around one of my opponent's legs in this position, but he has an easy ability in this situation as the legs are right next to each other to lock up his legs defensively.

That's not true in the case of the other hashiguramis. In a straight hashigurami, I'm between his legs. So it's hard for him to access one leg to the other because my body's in obstruction.

It's hard for him to get one leg to defend the other. In the case of a cross hashigurami, it makes no sense for him to cross his legs in this situation. It's actually helping me out.

He'd be doing me a favour if he did this. In this way, he's actually giving me the leg that I want. It makes much more sense for him now to split his legs and start the action of turning away.

That's really much more in his interest. So my opponent can't really use one leg to defend the other very successfully here. That gives me what I want.

I can relinquish control of the ankle briefly, but not the knee. The moment the knee is released, any kind of off-balancing results in a loss of the leg overall. So, with regards to stability, I can't be naive and just have my legs wide.

Now, a very interesting thing about reverse Ashi-Gurami is it starts to bring in different forms of leg lock than we've been looking at so far. When I was beginning in Jiu-Jitsu, leg lock mastery was always understood as who knew the most leg locks.

If you knew a hundred leg locks, you were more of a leg lock master than the guy who knew ten leg locks. A big part of my approach to leg locking has been to try to change that perception. If you look at my students, literally 95% of their leg lock submissions come from two moves, an inside heel hook and an outside heel hook.

They don't know a vast number of finishes. In truth, I could sit here all day and teach you literally hundreds of different exotic leg locks and it would do absolutely nothing to make you better at finishing people in top level competition. I would rather you had two high percentage leg locks that really work all the way up to world championship level than you have a thousand ineffective exotic leg locks that couldn't finish my grandmother.

The most important thing is my right knee will go inside my training partner's hip and thigh just like so. When he goes to turn to me, my knee makes it very very difficult for him and as a result, we can go through and catch our training partner at the ankle. We now have the perfect cross Ashigurami position with laced ankles.

When he tries to move from here, it's very very difficult. As we saw in the earlier segments, my real goal now will be to get to my training partner's primary leg at the heel and go into the heel hooking game. Initially, we can just control our training partner's legs and go into laced legs etc.

etc. You can go into attack Achilles locks, he walks on the secondary leg but ultimately, as we saw in the previous sequences or segments, we're looking to heel hook this leg. This is an easy way for us to go from one good position to an even better one.

(42:56 - 45:55)
Once again, working with my training partner here, we catch up and bring his foot over the top. I bring my knee in as a wedge. When he tries to recover his leg position, it's extraordinarily difficult from this position.

From here, it's not a difficult thing for us to go under and control and keep both legs in a position where it's hard for him to extend either leg. When I separate my training partner's legs, watch my right knee pop inside. My left knee goes inside my training partner's ankle like so and I hold on this two-on-one grip.

When he goes to pull away from me, it's shockingly difficult. You have excellent control of your training partner's legs. Initially, we bring the two legs in nice and close.

I could go directly for a lace leg attack here or, perhaps more realistically, we'll go into the various sequences that enable us to cover our training partner's heel and finish with inside heel hooks. Most of the time now, my partner will be trying to turn in this direction and we'll work as we saw in the earlier segments from this position. We've been looking at the broad structure of this leg lock system.

We've seen that really what people call the leg lock system in actuality is three subsystems that are linked together. When it's time to actually go out on the mat and wrestle someone and try to leg lock them, you're going to be using one of those three subsystems as your means of actually establishing contact with your opponent and going through to a win. A really interesting question is to ask, what's the relationship between the three subsystems? Obviously, structurally, they're very different.

Cross-Ashigurami has a very different mechanism of control and a very different mechanism of finishing from straight Ashigurami and you can say the same about reverse Ashigurami. Nonetheless, there are strong links between all three. Whenever people come to study leg locks with me, often they ask me one question which is, okay, your system is based around a hierarchy.

Why don't I just focus on the apex of the hierarchy? If the number one method of attack is the Cross-Ashigurami inside heel hook, why don't I just spend all my time on that? That's the best one. Well, the truth is, you're going to get the best results out of the leg lock system by having moves that work in unison to create dilemmas. It's very important we go over what is the nature of a dilemma and why is it so important for not only the leg lock system but Jiu-Jitsu in general.

I always say to my students, good Jiu-Jitsu players create problems for their opponents, but great Jiu-Jitsu players don't create problems, they create dilemmas. What's the difference? Every problem has a solution. Dilemmas don't really have solutions.

(45:56 - 47:23)
They're just two bad outcomes and you've got to make a choice between two undesirable outcomes. Whenever we go to work in the sport of Jiu-Jitsu, our task is not to create problems for our opponent but as often as possible to create dilemmas. The single most important dilemma in the leg lock system is the dilemma between the threat of outside heel hooks and the threat of inside heel hooks, between the threats that come from straight Ashi-Gurami and the threats that come from cross Ashi-Gurami.

The interplay between these two is what creates so many of the breakthroughs for my students. The only way you're going to have a devastating set of attacks for inside heel hooks is to be strong with your outside heel hooks and the only way you're going to have devastating attacks with your outside heel hooks is to have a strong viable threat of inside heel hooks. The two function in the same way that a jab and a cross function in boxing.

One sets up the other. You can only avoid one by exposing yourself to the other. If you only focus on one, you're going to quickly become predictable and your opponent will be able to position himself in certain ways that block your attacks.

But when you can threaten both back to back, your opponent's reaction to one will always open up the other. This is the basis of dilemma theory in the leg locking game. Let's start to look now in practical terms at how this plays out.

(47:26 - 47:35)
We know that our number one method of entering into our training partner's legs is the so-called Rimi Ashigarami. This is the basis of our entry pattern. I have an opponent on two knees.

(47:38 - 50:17)
From any method of entering in, let's say an elevation method, we get our opponent up and we go into an Rimi Ashigarami like so. Now, once we get into this position, the central guide as to where the action will go in the next few seconds is always betrayed by my opponent's head. The manner in which his head goes to move will determine how I go to attack the leg.

Many of your opponents threatened with this position will move the head away in an attempt to put a foot on the buttocks and scoot out and escape what they think is the threat of an initial outside heel hook. We saw when we looked at the outside heel hook system, this is not really a particularly effective way of operating. Whenever I see Kolmasara's head move away, look what happens to his ankle.

The ankle becomes exposed. Remember, we're always looking for some method of exposing our training partner's heels. The moment the head moves away, it can only happen in a way where it exposes my training partner's heel.

When I see the head move away, I simply cover the shoelaces with my bicep and lock up. From here, we switch from the Rimi Ashigarami and lock. When he tries to move away from me, I'm pointing my knees at the floor.

It's so easy now to apply pressure, roll through, make the switch to an outside Ashigarami, and now we're in a strong breaking position. So the first one of the dilemma was based on situations where my opponent's head moves away from me. If the head moves away, he will always expose himself at the heel and lead directly into the straight Ashigarami system, which involves a switch of various Ashigaramis, culminating in an outside Ashigarami and a strong breaking position.

Pretty soon, your opponent's going to figure that out. And they're going to realise that when the head moves away, the heel becomes exposed. So they're all going to do the same thing.

They're going to bring the head back to face us and put the heel on the floor. And as a result, there's no heel exposure. However, this comes at a very heavy price.

Yes, I don't have heel exposure, but I can always lock up the ankle. Now I put my left foot on my training partner's left buttock, the so-called cross buttock position. My right foot goes through and hooks onto the front of my training partner's quadriceps.

(50:18 - 50:27)
If I can, I always like to grip my training partner at the head. If my opponent's head is so far away that I can't do it, that's fine. I can do it without it.

(50:28 - 50:53)
Now, I want to lift and elevate my opponent by bringing my knees up to my chest. This will have an immediate destabilising effect on my training partner. As a result, his hips go directly over my head.

We now use what we call a hand assist. There's a danger of my opponent lifting his leg high, but I can't get my knee behind it as a wedge. A hand assist prevents this from happening.

(50:54 - 54:59)
I catch the knee, so when he goes to lift high, he can't do it. As a result, I can easily pull my knee behind his as a wedge. Now when he tries to rise, there's nowhere to go.

Now, we know that our next step is to break our training partner down to a hip. The correct directionality of force is always across the hip line. His basic support is his hands, but when I go across the line of hips, there's simply no basic support.

His ankles are tied together, and as a result, we can easily tilt our training partner down to the mat. What we've got here is a true dilemma. As I enter the Urumi Ashi-Gurami position, my opponent can either move away from me and expose his heel to the outside Ashi-Gurami system or square up to me and expose himself to the cross Ashi-Gurami system.

Let's look at some examples of this technique. For a situation where I'm tied with my training partner, and we draw him in, we go immediately into the legs. I see Kamisage here move away.

We roll through and finish. My opponent learns, he goes to square up to me. From here, I go cross body position, watch my right foot pommel through, and go to my training partner's hips.

If possible, I catch my training partner by the hip. If not, that's fine. Now, we bring knees to chest, and we bring our opponent's body in a situation where I can easily hand assist, catch, and put my knee behind his.

Now, I push along the line of the hips, sit my opponent down into a winning position. This simple dilemma, based on my opponent's head position, whether the head moves away or whether the head moves towards me, is the basis of how we use these two in unison to create attacking opportunities. My opponent has to go one or the other.

The head has to either move away from me or towards me. In either case, it leads into a devastating Ashi-Gurami based heel hook attack. When I see my opponent moving in towards me, it's so important that my foot goes across body position.

The second foot comes through and locks the tight grip on my training partner's quadriceps. This is essentially an X-Guard in reverse. Now, from here, even when my opponent puts his weight back and away from me, it's not a difficult thing for me to bring knees to chest.

That starts the ball rolling and gets him close to me. The last element is the extension of my legs that brings his hips directly over my head and my lower back off the floor. When he goes to pull away from me, I just hold the knee and the hand assist pulls him into a winning position.

From here, pushing across the hips creates a perfect attacking position. An ankle lace cross Ashi-Gurami is extremely difficult for my opponent to get away from. We're looking at the idea of bringing the notion of dilemmas into leg locking in particular and Jiu-Jitsu in general.

I always say this, good players create problems for their opponents, great players create dilemmas. We just looked at one of the foundational dilemmas of our leg lock system, based around our opponent's reaction, which is visible through their head position. When they move away from us, they're going to expose their own heel to the outside straight Ashi-Gurami game.

When their head moves towards us and they square up, they're going to expose their entire hip, both hips, to the cross Ashi-Gurami game. Let's look now at other ways in which dilemmas start to play an important role in our leg lock system. If we have an opponent in any given Ashi-Gurami, let's say for example cross Ashi-Gurami.

(55:01 - 1:01:57)
There's always going to be those times where you feel you're about to lose control of your opponent's knee as it slips out of your knee line. Say for example, I'm working here and Kama-Sai does a good job of turning and rotating and essentially now I've lost the position. The knee is now free.

Once again, from any situation where I have a given Ashi-Gurami on my opponent, here I'm demonstrating with cross Ashi-Gurami, I need to have his knee inside my knee line. He knows that and he will also be thumb-posting. Turn the other way.

Thumb-posting, pushing out, etc, etc. Whenever I feel I'm going to lose my training partner's leg on one side, it's up to me to create a dilemma by threatening the other side. So if I feel the knee is starting to come out and I'm losing control of it, I will often in these circumstances willingly put my foot out as the knee, as the leg continues out.

Now it's up to me to go into a very special grip. I thread my hand through and around and I palm-hold and I take a scoop grip on my training partner's leg. The key element to a scoop grip is that my hand goes above my training partner's knee and my elbow flares up to control my training partner's lower leg.

So the upper leg is controlled by hand and wrist and the lower leg is controlled by elbow. As Kama-Sai squares up to me and goes to pull away, walking back on his hands, walking back on his hands, look how far Kama-Sai has gotten away from me. It means nothing.

Even with his other foot pressing into the floor, I can always draw him back to me. This is a shockingly strong and intimidating grip. I'll do this again.

Even as Kama-Sai moves away from me, it looks like I'm completely losing control of his body, but because both upper body and lower body are well controlled, even when he pushes off the floor with hand, you can pull him back in like a child. Once again, he moves away. Now, once we get that pull, it's nothing special on my part.

It's just the structure of the human body. Just draw him back in. I want to do this with my legs in a better position.

So, I had a cross-Ashi-Urami situation with my training partner. He did a good job of escaping his knee from my knee line. I can see what's happening.

I'm losing the knee, just like a rocket science to figure this out. I put in my grip and step out. He goes to move away from me.

He thinks he's in safety because the knees are moving further and further away. He's moving away. He's all the way into my grip, almost gone, and yet it means nothing.

I can start to draw him in. As I draw him in, I put my foot to the inside position and I bring him right back into position where I can go on the attack. As he comes in, I like always in these circumstances to pass my foot over and switch my arms.

I keep my body high and as a result, we can make switches up into another one of our systems, the reverse-Ashi-Urami. Of course, on reverse-Ashi-Urami, we know what our opponent wants to do. He'll lock up his legs defensively.

We've seen it from situations like this. It's not difficult for us to fall down to the floor and go in to attack our opponent's leg. He goes to kick out and gives us the second leg for a kneebar win.

You can see there's a sense here in which your opponent can only escape one leg by exposing the other. The legs are so close to each other that it almost always means whenever I lose one leg, the other one is there for the taking. This creates incredible opportunities to get these simple dilemmas to work in your favour anytime you feel you're losing legs.

Here, we have a straight-Ashi-Urami situation. I feel Kama-Sara is doing a good job of pushing the foot down and starting to spread over my legs. As he comes in over my legs, there's always going to be exposure on the second leg.

From here, I can draw my training partner in towards me and I can go into an Ashi-Urami on the straight-Ashi-Urami on the second leg, or I can keep the scoop grip and from here, spin around my training partner here and go across-Ashi-Urami on the second leg. Your choice. Always bear in mind you've got two legs in front of you, not one.

So many athletes grab a hold of one leg and they get a tunnel vision where all they see is the leg in front of them. Never forget, he's got two legs. They're right next to each other.

If you lose one, just go to the other leg. Your best friend in any of these exchanges is the so-called scoop grip, which means that no matter how far your opponent gets away from you, you can always draw him back to you with minimal effort. No matter how many times he moves away, it's the easiest thing to bring him back.

So as I feel my opponent breaking my Ashi-Urami and stepping over the top, it's an easy thing for me to thread back into the second leg. It's easy because the two legs are right next to each other. I can work from here.

Let's look at one simple example. I lose Ashi-Urami on my partner's left leg. Then on the right, from here, we roll through and end up in a breaking position on the second leg.

This is another sense in which we can create simple dilemmas in almost any given leg lock situation. Not so much by a technical element, it's just a mental element of identifying the simple fact that the two legs are right next to each other. Whenever one escapes, the second is there for the taking.

We've gone over a large amount of theory with regards to the various elements of the general and subsystems that make up the entire leg lock system. It's time now to get into practical applications. Movements that we often use in training and competition to enter into the legs and to get to a decisive winning position.

In truth, I could sit here for months and show you a thousand different entries. What I want to do is to give you, in a practical sense, a sampler of ways to enter in practical fashions into leg locks from standing position, from ground positions on bottom, ground positions on top, and from a so-called double seated position where both assets are sitting down in front of each other. I can't show you the entire library of moves that we use in this position.

It simply wouldn't fit on any DVD series. But what I can do is show you some of the more practical ones which we use every day in training and use them to illustrate some of the concepts and tactics and insights which you can then employ in other areas in these four different contexts yourselves. Let's look now at one of my favourite ways to enter into the legs from standing position.

(1:01:58 - 1:05:33)
This particular method gained considerable attention when it was used very, very successfully by Gary Tonin in a spectacular battle of leg locking expertise between himself and the great Kuzma Palyaris. My concern as a coach when Gary went to fight Palyaris was the enormous discrepancy in size and strength. Palyaris is not only a very good leg locker, he's also very good at takedowns, especially lifting, slamming takedowns.

And one of my concerns was that if Gary got into upper body tie-ups with Mr. Palyaris, he would come off second best every single time. So I taught him what we call the sliding ashigurami entry, where from no tie-up whatsoever, neither athlete has a grip on the other, you slide into your legs. This is the equivalent in jiu-jitsu leg locking terms of a shot in wrestling, where you have no tie-up and you shoot from a distance for a takedown.

The grip we use is similar to a high crotch, but the end result is obviously very, very different. From a situation standing where I notice my opponent has his right foot forward and I also have my right foot forward, okay, so we have the same lead foot. I come off my training partner and I start by putting my head down just above the height of my training partner's hips.

I don't care if he stays high or if he comes down with me, it's going to make no difference, okay. The distance that I work with here is usually at a distance where I have hands on my training partner and I take a step and with a step I'll be able to reach the inside of my training partner's lead knee, okay. When I make my step, my chest comes down towards my lead leg, similar to a shot, but rather than shoot towards my knees, ultimately I want to shoot down onto my hips.

So what I'm going to do is I'm going to reach and I'm going to catch at my training partner's knee. How you catch is extremely important. I want to catch the whole tendon on the inside of the back of my training partner's knee, so my hand locks like so.

Make sure your fingers are together for a strong grip. I come in and there's the initial catch. When Kamosara goes to pull his leg back, it's shockingly difficult.

Now all I do is I put my hand right in front of my training partner's toes. As the hand catches the knee and the hand catches the toes, I now get an ability to slide. When I make that slide, I don't want to slide onto my back, I want to slide onto my side.

I have to make sure that I present enough space between our two feet that my hips can slide through that space. I don't want to step too close to my opponent's foot. Now there's no space for me to slide through.

So for an initial situation with my training partner, I come up to my right foot forward, often I'll post with my other hand. When I measure distance with my post hand, now I step onto my training partner's centre line. Off that step, the two hands catch and I slide onto my hip.

As I do that, I pull him forward onto me. Now we're in the perfect position, blocking the foot to throw our legs straight up into the ashi garami. As always, I look at my opponent's head.

If my opponent goes to a step in this direction, we attack, block and finish with outside ashi garami. I move down. From here, you can clearly see my training partner's centre line.

(1:05:33 - 1:06:45)
I step to the centre line. I use a post hand to enable me to get there. Now penetration step and a catch.

My chest goes down to catch, plant, watch my hip, I slide onto my side. I do not go onto my back. Then when I pull, he gets mounted.

Obviously that's not what we want. I pull in such a way that my head is outside his leg, so that when I pull, his body goes across me, not on top of me. Now from here, I throw my legs.

I look at his reaction. If the head moves away, we go ahead. If the head moves towards, we block, lift and put him down.

(1:14:05 - 1:15:31)
As a result, I can quickly fire up into the ashi gurami position. From here, I track my opponent's head, it moves away, the heel will be exposed, and we go into a winning position. Remember, our ultimate goal is always to get our head outside our opponent's leg.

If I fall in between my opponent's legs, there's a real danger of pulling him into bad positions. Okay, you are going into a bad guard fold. So it's very, very important I fall across my opponent's body, where his body weight goes across mine as opposed to on top of mine.

That means anytime his body goes across, it's the easiest thing in the world for me to go into a variation of ashi gurami in a winning position. Now, one of the interesting things about standing positions is we can often enter the legs in what are apparently disadvantageous positions. (11:43 - 12:13)

(19:10 - 20:08)

There are three characteristics of a successful elevation into any form of Ashi Garami, whether it be straight or cross, that you must master and understand. The first is hips overhead.

This is absolutely critical to your success when you go to elevate someone and you want to put them into the Ashi Garami game. (20:08 - 20:44)

That's the second key element. The third key element is knee position. My knee must dominate the space inside my opponent's head.

(20:44 - 21:59)

One of the things that I'm trying hard to push in this DVD series is that many of you already have strongly developed jiu-jitsu skills in other aspects of jiu-jitsu. A long-standing belief is that leg-locking, the ashi-gurama game, runs contrary to conventional jiu-jitsu.

That's a perception that I'm very much interested in changing. It's my belief that many highly talented athletes in the sport could easily add on the leg-locking game to their already existing skills and get great results in a very short period of time. The truth is, many of the already existent and well understood skills of the sport involve inside positioning.

These can easily be adapted into the leg-lock game so that someone who has good, strong, conventional skills within a very short time frame could become highly competent at leg-locking. (1:03:30 - 1:03:42)
We're looking at practical applications of all the leg lock theory we've seen up until this point. There are four main scenarios that you will be using entrances into leg locks. We've seen standing position.

(1:03:43 - 1:03:55)
We've seen ground position bottom. We're looking at ground position top. Let's look now to a fourth, very common scenario, especially once you start dealing more and more with leg locks and your training partners are, too.

(1:03:55 - 1:04:25)
When you first see this position, it looks kind of artificial, but once you start getting deep into the leg lock game, you'll find it occurring more and more often with you and your training partners. Even in regular jiu-jitsu competition, this position occurs all the time just because, in many cases, you'll have opponents who pull guard at the same time together, or one of them gets swept as they try to pass a guard, and the two athletes end up seated in front of each other. I often refer to this as the double-seated position.

(1:04:26 - 1:04:42)
Double-seated position refers to any situation where you have two athletes sitting in front of each other, and his knee line is here, my knee line is here. We are both outside of each other's knee line. Our knee lines have not crossed.

(1:04:43 - 1:04:49)
Here, I'm in a national garage. Our knee lines have crossed. We're that close.

(1:04:50 - 1:05:04)
In double-seated, we're not that close. There's still some distance between us, okay? In situations like this, if I'm interested in the leg lock game, I will always be looking to get my feet in the inside position. Currently, I've actually had that, I've achieved it.

(1:05:05 - 1:05:32)
But your opponent, if he's interested in leg locks, will be looking to do the same thing. So most of the time, when we start off in these situations, typically it'll be one leg for him inside and one leg for me inside. The question is, how am I gonna get two legs inside, or how am I gonna take advantage of this in some way if I have no legs inside? How am I gonna save myself instead of Sarah? What should I be trying to do in this double-seated position, okay? Well, the first thing I'm gonna do is put a brake on his ability to pommel his legs inside.

(1:05:33 - 1:05:55)
So the first thing I'll do is I'll take my hand and I will hug my opponent's foot to my thigh, okay? So if I have my opponent trying to pommel his leg inside, I don't grab my opponent's foot like this with my thumb and hold. The human hand is not strong enough to hold a leg. He will easily kick out and pommel his foot to the inside position.

(1:05:56 - 1:06:16)
Instead, what I do is I take an open hand and I hug his ankle to my thigh, and I use the strength of my extending leg to hold him in place. When he goes to pommel out, it's a damn sight more difficult than it was before. Presumably, he's holding my foot in the same way, okay? Now, to get inside position, I'm gonna take my hand and strip his grip.

(1:06:17 - 1:06:25)
As a result, I can pommel my foot to the inside. Where I pommel my foot to the inside is of the greatest importance. I have two choices.

(1:06:26 - 1:06:43)
I can either go straight buttock, my right foot to my opponent's left buttock. In this particular scenario, this is highly undesirable. Why? Because it creates a situation where there's a tremendous space between my knees, and my two knees are pointing in different directions.

(1:06:44 - 1:07:36)
